# QA Results for quiz-5-fixed-v3.md

## Q01

### Grounding

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no alternative shelter beds were available. <!-- correct -->

(b) Foot has a strong duress defense because the threat of freezing to death compelled her to continue operating the shelter, and a person of ordinary firmness would have yielded to the same pressure.

(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the MPC, the harm avoided need only be "greater than" the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that lower threshold.

(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she is automatically deemed at fault for creating the emergency solely by operating a shelter in a building that was later condemned, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.

(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the mere existence of general building safety codes and condemnation procedures categorically preempts the necessity defense by foreclosing any judicial balancing of harms.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Necessity is the correct defense here because Foot faces a choice of evils rather than coercion by a human actor. Under the common law (Fact 6), a defendant must show: (1) the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) no reasonable legal alternative exists, and (4) the defendant was not at fault in creating the emergency. These elements are satisfied here. Option (b) is incorrect because duress requires a threat of force from another person, not a natural condition like freezing weather. Option (c) is incorrect because Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, not the MPC. Option (d) is incorrect because lawfully operating a shelter prior to its condemnation does not automatically constitute culpable fault in creating the weather emergency itself. Option (e) is incorrect because general building codes are not the type of particularized, specific legislative choice required to preempt the necessity defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, necessity-proportionality, justification-vs-excuse], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse necessity (choice of evils) with duress (human coercion), or apply MPC standards in a CL jurisdiction

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Doctrinal Framework Part I (necessity elements); justification-vs-excuse refinement; necessity-proportionality-standard refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — necessity, Ch 21, Refinement `necessity-proportionality-standard`, `necessity-imminence`, `legislative-preemption-absolute`, `duress-threat-threshold`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no alternative shelter beds were available.

(b) Foot has a strong duress defense because the threat of freezing to death compelled her to continue operating the shelter, and a person of ordinary firmness would have yielded to the same pressure.

(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the MPC, the harm avoided need only be "greater than" the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that lower threshold.

(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she is automatically deemed at fault for creating the emergency solely by operating a shelter in a building that was later condemned, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.

(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the mere existence of general building safety codes and condemnation procedures categorically preempts the necessity defense by foreclosing any judicial balancing of harms.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Necessity is the correct defense here because Foot faces a choice of evils rather than coercion by a human actor. Under the common law (Fact 6), a defendant must show: (1) the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) no reasonable legal alternative exists, and (4) the defendant was not at fault in creating the emergency. These elements are satisfied here. Option (b) is incorrect because duress requires a threat of force from another person, not a natural condition like freezing weather. Option (c) is incorrect because Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, not the MPC. Option (d) is incorrect because lawfully operating a shelter prior to its condemnation does not automatically constitute culpable fault in creating the weather emergency itself. Option (e) is incorrect because general building codes are not the type of particularized, specific legislative choice required to preempt the necessity defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, necessity-proportionality, justification-vs-excuse], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse necessity (choice of evils) with duress (human coercion), or apply MPC standards in a CL jurisdiction

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Doctrinal Framework Part I (necessity elements); justification-vs-excuse refinement; necessity-proportionality-standard refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies necessity as the primary defense when a defendant faces a "choice of evils" brought about by natural forces like life-threatening cold. Foot can demonstrate that the preservation of twelve human lives "clearly outweighs" the technical harm of violating a building condemnation order. The defense is robust here because the threat was imminent (the cold was immediate) and there were no reasonable legal alternatives (no other shelter beds available). Under the common law standard mentioned in Fact 6, these elements establish a prima facie justification.

(b) Argument-for: While duress usually involves human threats, a student could argue that the "pressure" of the situation effectively functioned as a surrogate for human coercion. The defense of duress focuses on the defendant's state of mind and whether a person of "ordinary firmness" would have been unable to resist the pressure to break the law. If one views the weather as an irresistible force that "compelled" Foot's actions to prevent death, duress provides a conceptually similar excuse-based framework to necessity. This argument appeals to the shared underlying principle of avoiding greater harm under extreme pressure.

(c) Argument-for: This argument focuses on the proportionality element of the necessity defense. Under the Model Penal Code (MPC) § 3.02, the defendant is justified if the harm or evil sought to be avoided is "greater than" that sought to be prevented by the law. This is a more lenient standard than the common law’s "clearly outweighs" requirement. If the jurisdiction followed the MPC, the defense would be even stronger because the balancing test is weighted more heavily in the defendant's favor, and protecting twelve lives is indisputably a "greater" good than maintaining a condemnation order.

(d) Argument-for: A common law necessity defense is unavailable if the defendant was at fault in "bringing about" the situation that required the choice of evils. A prosecutor could argue that Foot created the emergency by choosing to house people in a substandard building that was susceptible to condemnation. By operating an unsafe facility, she placed herself in the position where she would eventually have to choose between illegal occupancy and the elements. Under this strict "fault" interpretation, her initial negligence in building management bars her from later claiming necessity as a shield.

(e) Argument-for: The necessity defense is a "gap-filler" that yields to legislative intent. If a legislature has already balanced the competing values—here, the danger of unsafe buildings versus the need for shelter—and created a comprehensive regulatory scheme (building codes and condemnation procedures), the court may not substitute its own "choice of evils" balancing. This argument posits that the existence of the code itself represents the legislature's final word on when a building is too dangerous to occupy, effectively preempting a judicial necessity defense for safety-code violations.

Head-to-head: (a) is the strongest because it correctly identifies the applicable doctrinal framework (common law necessity) and matches the facts (natural threat, no alternatives). (b) is legally incorrect because duress requires a human threat. (c) is legally incorrect because the facts specify a common law jurisdiction, not MPC. (d) and (e) fail because they rely on overly broad "absolute" triggers; "automatically" and "categorically" are incorrect descriptions of how the fault bar and legislative preemption work in practice. The common law does not bar necessity solely because of prior negligence if that negligence didn't "create" the weather emergency, nor do general codes categorically preempt the defense for specific, unforeseen life-safety crises.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "Foot has a strong duress defense because the threat of freezing to death..." — wrong because duress requires a threat from a human being, not natural conditions.
- (c): "under the MPC... that lower threshold." — wrong because Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law.
- (d): "automatically deemed at fault... solely by operating a shelter..." — wrong because operating a lawful business that is later condemned is not per se "fault" in creating a weather emergency.
- (e): "categorically preempts the necessity defense by foreclosing any judicial balancing..." — wrong because general safety codes do not manifest the specific legislative intent to exclude necessity defenses in extreme life-or-death scenarios.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no alternative shelter beds were available. <!-- correct -->

(b) Foot has a strong duress defense because the threat of freezing to death compelled her to continue operating the shelter, and a person of ordinary firmness would have yielded to the same pressure.

(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the MPC, the harm avoided need only be "greater than" the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that lower threshold.

(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she is automatically deemed at fault for creating the emergency solely by operating a shelter in a building that was later condemned, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.

(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the mere existence of general building safety codes and condemnation procedures categorically preempts the necessity defense by foreclosing any judicial balancing of harms.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Necessity is the correct defense here because Foot faces a choice of evils rather than coercion by a human actor. Under the common law (Fact 6), a defendant must show: (1) the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) no reasonable legal alternative exists, and (4) the defendant was not at fault in creating the emergency. These elements are satisfied here. Option (b) is incorrect because duress requires a threat of force from another person, not a natural condition like freezing weather. Option (c) is incorrect because Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, not the MPC. Option (d) is incorrect because lawfully operating a shelter prior to its condemnation does not automatically constitute culpable fault in creating the weather emergency itself. Option (e) is incorrect because general building codes are not the type of particularized, specific legislative choice required to preempt the necessity defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, necessity-proportionality, justification-vs-excuse], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse necessity (choice of evils) with duress (human coercion), or apply MPC standards in a CL jurisdiction

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Doctrinal Framework Part I (necessity elements); justification-vs-excuse refinement; necessity-proportionality-standard refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the choice-of-evils (necessity) defense as the proper doctrine for natural emergencies. It accurately maps the classic common law elements—proportionality (harm avoided clearly outweighs harm caused), imminence, and lack of alternatives—to the specific facts (saving twelve people from freezing vs. occupying a condemned building).
(b) Argument-for: A student might argue that extreme weather exerted an overwhelming coercive force that a "person of ordinary firmness" could not resist, mapping the language of psychological compulsion onto the facts. The threat to life was profound, satisfying the gravity requirement of a compulsion-based excuse.
(c) Argument-for: This option accurately states the Model Penal Code standard for necessity, which rejects the strict common law "imminence" requirement and simply asks whether the harm avoided was "greater than" the harm caused. Saving twelve lives is objectively a greater good than obeying a local condemnation order.
(d) Argument-for: Under the common law, a defendant who is at fault in bringing about the emergency cannot claim necessity. A student could plausibly argue that by maintaining a substandard building that ultimately required condemnation, Foot created the underlying conditions that forced her into the illegal act, thereby foreclosing the defense.
(e) Argument-for: Necessity is generally unavailable when the legislature has already weighed the competing values. One could argue that by enacting building codes and establishing condemnation procedures, the legislature categorically decided that occupying a condemned building is intolerably dangerous, preempting ad-hoc judicial balancing.

Head-to-head: Option (a) cleanly applies the correct doctrine (necessity) and the correct jurisdictional standard (common law) to the facts. Option (b) fails doctrinally because it asserts a duress defense based on natural conditions (freezing weather), whereas duress strictly requires a human threat. Option (c) is factually incorrect because it applies the MPC standard, directly contradicting Fact 6. Options (d) and (e) are effectively neutralized by absolute locking words ("automatically," "solely," "categorically"); common law does not automatically assign emergency-creating fault merely for operating a building that gets condemned, nor do general building codes explicitly and categorically preempt the necessity defense in life-or-death emergencies.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "strong duress defense because the threat of freezing to death" — wrong because duress requires a threat of force from a human actor, not natural environmental conditions.
- (c): "under the MPC" — wrong because Fact 6 specifies that the jurisdiction applies the common law, not the Model Penal Code.
- (d): "automatically deemed at fault... solely by operating a shelter" — wrong because operating a building prior to its condemnation does not legally constitute automatic, per se fault for the subsequent freezing weather emergency.
- (e): "categorically preempts the necessity defense" — wrong because general building and safety codes do not constitute the specific, deliberate legislative preemption of the choice-of-evils defense required to foreclose judicial balancing in extreme emergencies.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q02

### Grounding

**Q2.** Your supervisor asks whether Foot's necessity defense has any impact on the potential liability of volunteers who helped Foot keep the shelter open during the standoff (Facts 1 and 6). Assuming common law applies, which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers have no defense because necessity is a personal excuse that applies only to the person who made the choice of evils, and each volunteer would need to independently establish their own duress defense.
(b) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers who assisted her share in the justification because necessity is a justification—the act itself is deemed right—and any third party who knowingly assists in a justified act is also justified. <!-- correct -->
(c) The volunteers are liable as accomplices regardless of Foot's defense because the common law treats accomplice liability as strict—once a person knowingly assists in a criminal act, no defense available to the principal transfers to the accomplice.
(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each volunteer independently demonstrated they had exhausted all legal alternatives before joining the shelter operation, because necessity's "no reasonable legal alternative" element must be proven individually by each participant.
(e) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers share in the defense, but only because the MPC extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices as a matter of statutory design, not because of the justification principle.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** The justification/excuse distinction has concrete consequences for derivative liability. Necessity is a justification—the defendant's act is deemed the right choice under the circumstances. When an act is justified, the justification attaches to the act itself. Any third party who knowingly assists in a justified act shares in that justification. Option (a) misclassifies necessity as an excuse; excuses are personal to the actor. Option (c) relies on a false rule; when the principal's act is justified, there is no wrongful act for accomplice liability to attach. Option (d) incorrectly states that derivative justification requires each helper to independently satisfy every element. Option (e) improperly applies the MPC (the question specifies common law) and substantively misstates MPC law, as the MPC does not extend all affirmative defenses (such as personal excuses) to accomplices.

**Tags:** chapters: [18, 21], topics: [justification-vs-excuse, necessity, derivative-liability], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students classify necessity as an excuse and deny volunteers a derivative defense

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, justification-vs-excuse refinement; Chapter 18, dl-justification-vs-excuse.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — justification-vs-excuse distinction in derivative liability, Ch 18, Refinement `dl-justification-vs-excuse`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Edge Case Audit

Assuming common law applies, which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers have no defense because necessity is a personal excuse that applies only to the person who made the choice of evils, and each volunteer would need to independently establish their own duress defense.
(b) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers who assisted her share in the justification because necessity is a justification—the act itself is deemed right—and any third party who knowingly assists in a justified act is also justified. <!-- correct -->
(c) The volunteers are liable as accomplices regardless of Foot's defense because the common law treats accomplice liability as strict—once a person knowingly assists in a criminal act, no defense available to the principal transfers to the accomplice.
(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each volunteer independently demonstrated they had exhausted all legal alternatives before joining the shelter operation, because necessity's "no reasonable legal alternative" element must be proven individually by each participant.

Any third party who knowingly assists in a justified act shares in that justification. Option (a) misclassifies necessity as an excuse; excuses are personal to the actor. Option (c) relies on a false rule; when the principal's act is justified, there is no wrongful act for accomplice liability to attach. Option (d) incorrectly states that derivative justification requires each helper to independently satisfy every element. ### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

Q2. Your supervisor asks whether Foot's necessity defense has any impact on the potential liability of volunteers who helped Foot keep the shelter open during the standoff (Facts 1 and 6). Assuming common law applies, which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers have no defense because necessity is a personal excuse that applies only to the person who made the choice of evils, and each volunteer would need to independently establish their own duress defense.
(b) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers who assisted her share in the justification because necessity is a justification—the act itself is deemed right—and any third party who knowingly assists in a justified act is also justified. <!-- correct -->
(c) The volunteers are liable as accomplices regardless of Foot's defense because the common law treats accomplice liability as strict—once a person knowingly assists in a criminal act, no defense available to the principal transfers to the accomplice.
(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each volunteer independently demonstrated they had exhausted all legal alternatives before joining the shelter operation, because necessity's "no reasonable legal alternative" element must be proven individually by each participant.
(e) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers share in the defense, but only because the MPC extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices as a matter of statutory design, not because of the justification principle.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** The justification/excuse distinction has concrete consequences for derivative liability. Necessity is a justification—the defendant's act is deemed the right choice under the circumstances. When an act is justified, the justification attaches to the act itself. Any third party who knowingly assists in a justified act shares in that justification. Option (a) misclassifies necessity as an excuse; excuses are personal to the actor. Option (c) relies on a false rule; when the principal's act is justified, there is no wrongful act for accomplice liability to attach. Option (d) incorrectly states that derivative justification requires each helper to independently satisfy every element. Option (e) improperly applies the MPC (the question specifies common law) and substantively misstates MPC law, as the MPC does not extend all affirmative defenses (such as personal excuses) to accomplices.

**Tags:** chapters: [18, 21], topics: [justification-vs-excuse, necessity, derivative-liability], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students classify necessity as an excuse and deny volunteers a derivative defense

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, justification-vs-excuse refinement; Chapter 18, dl-justification-vs-excuse.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Historically, the distinction between necessity and duress has been blurred, and some older common law traditions or minority views treat necessity as an excuse. If necessity is categorized as an excuse, it implies the act was wrongful but the actor's willpower was overborne by circumstances. Because excuses are personal to the actor's state of mind, they do not transfer to third parties. Under this view, Foot's volunteers would not benefit from her defense and would instead need to establish their own individual defenses, such as duress, based on their own specific circumstances.

(b) Argument-for: At common law, necessity is defined as a justification because it represents a "choice of evils" where the defendant chose the lesser of two harms, making the act the socially correct one. Because the act itself is deemed "right" or "lawful," there is no underlying criminal act to which accomplice liability can attach. Accomplice liability is derivative; if the principal’s conduct is justified, it is not a crime, and therefore those who knowingly assist in that conduct cannot be guilty of aiding a crime. The volunteers effectively join in the "right" act and are thus shielded by the same justification as Foot.

(c) Argument-for: This argument posits that accomplice liability should be viewed as an independent assessment of an individual's decision to support a prohibited act. Under a strict interpretation of derivative liability, the focus is on whether the accomplice intended to assist in the physical acts that constitute the crime's elements. If defenses are viewed as specific immunities granted to individuals by the court for equitable reasons, they would not naturally extend to accomplices. This approach maintains a high bar for law-breaking, ensuring that volunteers are held accountable for their own choices to assist in a standoff, regardless of the principal's unique defenses.

(d) Argument-for: The "no reasonable legal alternative" element is a cornerstone of the necessity defense and requires a subjective and objective evaluation of the options available to the actor. One could argue that even if Foot had no alternatives, a volunteer might have had many legal ways to help the cause without engaging in a criminal standoff. Because each person makes an independent choice to participate in the illegal activity, the necessity of that participation must be proven for each individual. Under this logic, Foot's justification only covers her helpers if they also faced the same lack of alternatives that she did.

(e) Argument-for: This option suggests that the common law is restrictive regarding the transfer of defenses and that the modern tendency to protect accomplices is actually a result of statutory frameworks like the Model Penal Code. By arguing that the "justification principle" is a secondary or non-existent concept at common law, a student could claim that any protection for the volunteers must come from a specific statutory grant. This views the extension of Foot’s defense as a matter of modern legal policy rather than an inherent feature of common law justification doctrine.

Head-to-head: The core of the question is the distinction between justifications (which attach to the act) and excuses (which attach to the actor). Option (b) correctly identifies necessity as a justification and applies the common law rule that justifications transfer to accomplices. Option (a) fails by mislabeling necessity as an excuse. Option (c) fails by claiming accomplice liability is "strict" and that no defenses transfer, which contradicts the fundamental derivative nature of the crime and the transferability of justifications. Option (d) fails because if an act is justified, the act itself is not a crime; the "no legal alternative" element applies to the choice to perform the act, not to each individual’s decision to assist in a lawful act. Option (e) fails by invoking the MPC when the question specifies common law and by falsely stating that the MPC extends "all" affirmative defenses (it does not extend personal excuses like insanity to accomplices). Option (b) is the only one that correctly pairs the classification of the defense with its legal effect at common law.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "necessity is a personal excuse" — wrong because common law necessity is traditionally a justification, not an excuse.
- (c): "common law treats accomplice liability as strict" — wrong because accomplice liability requires specific intent (it is not a strict liability offense) and justifications transfer to accomplices.
- (d): "necessity's 'no reasonable legal alternative' element must be proven individually by each participant" — wrong because if an act is justified, the act is not wrongful for any participant.
- (e): "the MPC extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices" — wrong because the MPC does not extend personal excuses to accomplices, and the question specifies common law.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q2.** Your supervisor asks whether Foot's necessity defense has any impact on the potential liability of volunteers who helped Foot keep the shelter open during the standoff (Facts 1 and 6). Assuming common law applies, which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers have no defense because necessity is a personal excuse that applies only to the person who made the choice of evils, and each volunteer would need to independently establish their own duress defense.
(b) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers who assisted her share in the justification because necessity is a justification—the act itself is deemed right—and any third party who knowingly assists in a justified act is also justified. <!-- correct -->
(c) The volunteers are liable as accomplices regardless of Foot's defense because the common law treats accomplice liability as strict—once a person knowingly assists in a criminal act, no defense available to the principal transfers to the accomplice.
(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each volunteer independently demonstrated they had exhausted all legal alternatives before joining the shelter operation, because necessity's "no reasonable legal alternative" element must be proven individually by each participant.
(e) If Foot's necessity defense succeeds, the volunteers share in the defense, but only because the MPC extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices as a matter of statutory design, not because of the justification principle.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** The justification/excuse distinction has concrete consequences for derivative liability. Necessity is a justification—the defendant's act is deemed the right choice under the circumstances. When an act is justified, the justification attaches to the act itself. Any third party who knowingly assists in a justified act shares in that justification. Option (a) misclassifies necessity as an excuse; excuses are personal to the actor. Option (c) relies on a false rule; when the principal's act is justified, there is no wrongful act for accomplice liability to attach. Option (d) incorrectly states that derivative justification requires each helper to independently satisfy every element. Option (e) improperly applies the MPC (the question specifies common law) and substantively misstates MPC law, as the MPC does not extend all affirmative defenses (such as personal excuses) to accomplices.

**Tags:** chapters: [18, 21], topics: [justification-vs-excuse, necessity, derivative-liability], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students classify necessity as an excuse and deny volunteers a derivative defense

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, justification-vs-excuse refinement; Chapter 18, dl-justification-vs-excuse.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that necessity, often taught alongside duress, is based on the actor's overwhelming pressure to act, making it an excuse rather than a justification. Because excuses are personal to the specific actor facing the pressure, they do not transfer to accomplices. Therefore, the volunteers would need to independently establish their own excuse (such as duress) rather than relying on Foot's necessity defense.
(b) Argument-for: This option correctly reflects the common law distinction between justification and excuse. Necessity is the quintessential justification defense, meaning the act itself is deemed legally permissible or beneficial. Because the act is justified, there is no legally wrongful conduct, so anyone who assists the principal in performing that justified act derivatively shares in the defense and cannot be held liable as an accomplice.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that accomplice liability solely focuses on whether the accomplice intended to and did assist the principal's factual conduct. From this perspective, the common law rigidly separates the accomplice's act of aiding from the principal's affirmative defenses, making the accomplice strictly liable for assisting the proscribed act regardless of whether the principal's conduct was ultimately excused or justified.
(d) Argument-for: A student might argue that because criminal liability assesses individual culpability, derivative defenses still require individual satisfaction of the defense's elements. Since necessity requires the actor to exhaust all legal alternatives, the volunteers cannot simply piggyback on Foot's efforts; they must each prove they personally lacked reasonable legal alternatives before choosing to break the law.
(e) Argument-for: A student might argue that modern criminal law, heavily influenced by the Model Penal Code, rejects the strict common law justification/excuse dichotomy for accomplices. Under this view, the MPC explicitly extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices to ensure fairness, and this statutory structure provides the actual reason the volunteers share the defense, overriding theoretical justification principles.

Head-to-head: Option (b) correctly states the common law rule that necessity is a justification, which attaches to the act itself and protects accomplices. Option (a) incorrectly classifies necessity as an excuse. Option (c) invents a false rule that no defenses transfer to accomplices, ignoring the justification/excuse distinction. Option (d) incorrectly states that accomplices to a justified act must independently prove the justification's elements (such as exhausting alternatives) to share in the defense. Option (e) both misstates the MPC (which does not extend excuses like duress to accomplices) and ignores the prompt's explicit instruction to apply the common law. All distractors contain clear, falsifiable legal errors.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "necessity is a personal excuse" — wrong because common law categorically classifies necessity as a justification defense, not a personal excuse.
- (c): "no defense available to the principal transfers to the accomplice" — wrong because common law dictates that justification defenses (which render the act legally permissible) categorically transfer to accomplices.
- (d): "necessity's 'no reasonable legal alternative' element must be proven individually by each participant" — wrong because when an act is justified, the justification attaches to the act itself; accomplices do not need to independently satisfy the necessity elements to derive the defense.
- (e): "the MPC extends all affirmative defenses to accomplices as a matter of statutory design" — wrong because the MPC does not extend personal excuses to accomplices, and it also ignores the prompt's explicit constraint to assume common law applies.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q03

### Grounding

**Q3.** Your supervisor turns to Holmes's liability for shoving Lisa Chen (Fact 3). Holmes claims self-defense. Under the common law standard of self-defense (as applied in *People v. Goetz*), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because he honestly believed Lisa was part of a forced-removal team, and an honest belief in the need for force is sufficient to establish self-defense under the common law.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because a reasonable person in Holmes's situation—even after hearing Foot's warning about forced removal—would not have believed force was necessary to defend against an imminent use of unlawful force when faced with a small, unarmed woman identifying herself as a process server. <!-- correct -->

(c) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because Foot's earlier statement that "the city is going to send people to drag us out" constitutes the kind of prior threat information that automatically makes any subsequent belief in danger objectively reasonable.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim solely because he failed to retreat before using force, and the common law imposes a duty to retreat before any use of force.

(e) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because his prior conviction for assault makes him a "person of violent character" who is categorically barred from raising self-defense under the common law.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Self-defense requires an objectively reasonable belief that force was necessary against an imminent use of unlawful force. The standard asks what a reasonable person in the defendant's situation would have believed (*People v. Goetz*). Holmes's "situation" legally incorporates his knowledge of Foot's prior warning. However, a reasonable person who hears a warning about forced removal, then encounters a small, unarmed woman who verbally identifies herself as a process server, would not conclude that an imminent use of unlawful force is underway. Therefore, his belief was objectively unreasonable. Option (a) is incorrect because the common law requires objective reasonableness, not merely an honest (purely subjective) belief. Option (c) is incorrect because while prior threat information is incorporated into the circumstances, it does not "automatically" make a subsequent belief objectively reasonable. Option (d) is incorrect because the common law duty to retreat applies only before using deadly force, and Holmes used non-deadly force. Option (e) is incorrect because a prior assault conviction does not categorically bar a defendant from raising a self-defense claim.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, imminence], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students apply a purely subjective standard or assume any prior experience makes the belief reasonable

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, objective-reasonableness-standard refinement; People v. Goetz.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — self-defense, Ch 22, Refinement `objective-reasonableness-standard` and `situational-incorporation`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q3.** Your supervisor turns to Holmes's liability for shoving Lisa Chen (Fact 3). Holmes claims self-defense. Under the common law standard of self-defense (as applied in *People v. Goetz*), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because he honestly believed Lisa was part of a forced-removal team, and an honest belief in the need for force is sufficient to establish self-defense under the common law.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because a reasonable person in Holmes's situation—even after hearing Foot's warning about forced removal—would not have believed force was necessary to defend against an imminent use of unlawful force when faced with a small, unarmed woman identifying herself as a process server.

(c) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because Foot's earlier statement that "the city is going to send people to drag us out" constitutes the kind of prior threat information that automatically makes any subsequent belief in danger objectively reasonable.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim solely because he failed to retreat before using force, and the common law imposes a duty to retreat before any use of force.

(e) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because his prior conviction for assault makes him a "person of violent character" who is categorically barred from raising self-defense under the common law.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Self-defense requires an objectively reasonable belief that force was necessary against an imminent use of unlawful force. The standard asks what a reasonable person in the defendant's situation would have believed (*People v. Goetz*). Holmes's "situation" legally incorporates his knowledge of Foot's prior warning. However, a reasonable person who hears a warning about forced removal, then encounters a small, unarmed woman who verbally identifies herself as a process server, would not conclude that an imminent use of unlawful force is underway. Therefore, his belief was objectively unreasonable. Option (a) is incorrect because the common law requires objective reasonableness, not merely an honest (purely subjective) belief. Option (c) is incorrect because while prior threat information is incorporated into the circumstances, it does not "automatically" make a subsequent belief objectively reasonable. Option (d) is incorrect because the common law duty to retreat applies only before using deadly force, and Holmes used non-deadly force. Option (e) is incorrect because a prior assault conviction does not categorically bar a defendant from raising a self-defense claim.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, imminence], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students apply a purely subjective standard or assume any prior experience makes the belief reasonable

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, objective-reasonableness-standard refinement; People v. Goetz.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Self-defense requires that the defendant possess a genuine, honest belief that force is necessary to protect themselves. If Holmes genuinely believed he was being targeted by a forced-removal team, he lacked the mens rea for an unprovoked assault. Under a purely subjective interpretation of common law principles, the honesty of the defendant's fear is the primary moral inquiry. Therefore, if his fear was sincere, the defense should be available to negate criminal culpability.

(b) Argument-for: Under the *People v. Goetz* standard, self-defense requires both a subjective belief and an objective one. Even if Holmes honestly feared a "forced removal," the objective prong asks what a reasonable person in his situation would believe. A reasonable person, seeing a small, unarmed woman who has explicitly identified herself as a process server, would recognize that she does not pose an imminent threat of unlawful physical force. Because the belief fails the objective reasonableness test, the defense is unavailable.

(c) Argument-for: *People v. Goetz* establishes that the "reasonable person" is placed in the defendant's specific situation, which includes the defendant’s knowledge and prior experiences. Foot’s warning about a city-sanctioned "forced removal" creates a state of heightened, justified alertness. In this specific context, the arrival of any stranger attempting to engage Holmes validates his suspicion. Prior threat information serves as the foundational evidence that transforms a subjective fear into a reasonable reaction based on the "situation" known to the actor.

(d) Argument-for: The common law traditionally favors the preservation of peace and requires individuals to avoid physical altercations if possible. In many iterations of common law doctrine, a defendant must exhaust all safe avenues of withdrawal before resorting to physical force. Since Holmes was in a position where he could have simply closed his door or ignored the process server rather than initiating physical contact, his failure to retreat precludes the necessity required for a self-defense claim.

(e) Argument-for: Justification defenses like self-defense are rooted in the "innocence" of the actor. In some legal traditions and evidentiary frameworks, a defendant with a demonstrated history of violent behavior—such as a prior assault conviction—is viewed as the likely aggressor or as someone who has forfeited the benefit of the doubt regarding the "reasonableness" of their violent reactions. If the law categorizes Holmes as a violent character, his claim of acting in defensive "necessity" is legally undermined by his established propensity for violence.

Head-to-head: (b) is the strongest because it correctly identifies the two-pronged test from *Goetz* (subjective and objective) and applies the objective "reasonable person" standard to the specific facts (small, unarmed, identifying herself). (a) is weak because it ignores the objective requirement central to *Goetz*. (c) is weak because no legal doctrine makes prior information "automatically" satisfy the reasonableness requirement; it is merely one factor for the jury. (d) is weak because the duty to retreat almost exclusively applies to deadly force, whereas a shove is non-deadly force. (e) is weak because a prior conviction is an evidentiary issue, not a categorical bar to a substantive justification defense.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "an honest belief in the need for force is sufficient to establish self-defense" — wrong because *Goetz* and common law require the belief to be objectively reasonable as well.
- (c): "automatically makes any subsequent belief in danger objectively reasonable" — wrong because reasonableness is a factual determination for the jury, not a legal automaticity triggered by prior threats.
- (d): "the common law imposes a duty to retreat before any use of force" — wrong because the duty to retreat applies only to the use of deadly force.
- (e): "categorically barred from raising self-defense under the common law" — wrong because a prior conviction does not remove one's legal right to defend oneself from an actual imminent attack.

Recommended fix: none
-->

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that under a purely subjective mens rea approach, an honest belief in the necessity of force justifies the conduct by negating the intent to commit a crime. If Holmes experienced genuine terror upon seeing an intruder after Foot's warning, his honest subjective belief means he lacked the culpable mental state for battery or assault, satisfying self-defense.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that *People v. Goetz* requires an objectively reasonable belief in the necessity of force, considering the circumstances as they appeared to the defendant. While Holmes's situation included the prior warning, the objective component remains: a reasonable person would observe that the intruder is a small, unarmed woman who expressly identified herself as a process server. - (a): "an honest belief in the need for force is sufficient to establish self-defense under the common law" — wrong because *Goetz* and common law require the belief to also be objectively reasonable.
- (c): "automatically makes any subsequent belief in danger objectively reasonable" — wrong because prior threats are context for the jury's reasonableness determination, but do not automatically or categorically render any subsequent fear reasonable.

## Q04

### Grounding

**Q4.** Returning to this jurisdiction's default rules (Fact 6), your supervisor asks about Holmes's claim that he stole the $5,000 under duress (Fact 4). Under the common law, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because Nietzsche threatened serious bodily injury to Holmes's mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's history, and the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline that places the threatened harm days in the future.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress requires a threat directed at the defendant personally, and the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members.

(c) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was not imminent -- Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, and the common law requires a present and immediate threat, not one that will materialize days later. <!-- correct -->

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law, which limits the defense to offenses against persons.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense because a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific, and Nietzsche's history of violence makes the threat both credible and specific enough to satisfy the common law standard.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the common law (Fact 6), duress requires (1) a threat of death or serious bodily injury, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) the threat targets the defendant or a close relation, and (4) no reasonable legal alternative exists. Nietzsche's threat to "hurt her bad" satisfies the threat threshold and the target requirement (Holmes's mother is a close relation). However, the threat lacks imminence under the common law's strict standard. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday -- multiple days away. The common law requires a "present and immediate" threat, not one that will materialize days later. Option (a) falsely claims that the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats. This conflates the strict common-law rule with the *Dingwall* relaxation; under the common law, imminence requires a present and immediate threat, and a pattern of past violence does not substitute for temporal proximity. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, defeating the imminence element. Option (b) states that the common law "does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. The common law extends duress to threats against the defendant or a close relation, and a mother is a close relation. Option (d) fabricates a crime-type limitation -- the false proposition is that "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes." The common law murder bar applies only to homicide; duress is available for property crimes. Option (e) states that "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" -- the false proposition is this restatement of the imminence rule. The common law requires imminence as a separate element; credibility and specificity do not substitute for temporal immediacy.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: A documented pattern of carrying out past threats satisfies CL imminence regardless of any future deadline — wrong because CL requires a present and immediate threat; pattern-of-past-violence does not substitute for temporal proximity, and "until Friday" defeats imminence
(b) False claim: CL does not extend duress to threats against family members — wrong because CL extends to threats against close relations
(c) CORRECT
(d) False claim: Duress is categorically unavailable for theft — wrong because the CL bar applies only to homicide
(e) False claim: Credibility and specificity substitute for imminence — wrong because CL treats imminence as a mandatory separate element
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence, duress-threat-threshold, duress-target-scope], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and family relationship while overlooking that "until Friday" defeats CL imminence

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-threat-threshold refinement; duress-imminence-proximity refinement (CL requires imminent threat); Doctrinal Framework Part II.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Duress, Ch 21, Refinements `duress-imminence-proximity` and `duress-target-scope`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q4.** Returning to this jurisdiction's default rules (Fact 6), your supervisor asks about Holmes's claim that he stole the $5,000 under duress (Fact 4). Under the common law, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because Nietzsche threatened serious bodily injury to Holmes's mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's history, and the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline that places the threatened harm days in the future.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress requires a threat directed at the defendant personally, and the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members.

(c) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was not imminent -- Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, and the common law requires a present and immediate threat, not one that will materialize days later. <!-- correct -->

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law, which limits the defense to offenses against persons.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense because a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific, and Nietzsche's history of violence makes the threat both credible and specific enough to satisfy the common law standard.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the common law (Fact 6), duress requires (1) a threat of death or serious bodily injury, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) the threat targets the defendant or a close relation, and (4) no reasonable legal alternative exists. Nietzsche's threat to "hurt her bad" satisfies the threat threshold and the target requirement (Holmes's mother is a close relation). However, the threat lacks imminence under the common law's strict standard. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday -- multiple days away. The common law requires a "present and immediate" threat, not one that will materialize days later. Option (a) falsely claims that the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats. This conflates the strict common-law rule with the *Dingwall* relaxation; under the common law, imminence requires a present and immediate threat, and a pattern of past violence does not substitute for temporal proximity. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, defeating the imminence element. Option (b) states that the common law "does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. The common law extends duress to threats against the defendant or a close relation, and a mother is a close relation. Option (d) fabricates a crime-type limitation -- the false proposition is that "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes." The common law murder bar applies only to homicide; duress is available for property crimes. Option (e) states that "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" -- the false proposition is this restatement of the imminence rule. The common law requires imminence as a separate element; credibility and specificity do not substitute for temporal immediacy.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: A documented pattern of carrying out past threats satisfies CL imminence regardless of any future deadline — wrong because CL requires a present and immediate threat; pattern-of-past-violence does not substitute for temporal proximity, and "until Friday" defeats imminence
(b) False claim: CL does not extend duress to threats against family members — wrong because CL extends to threats against close relations
(c) CORRECT
(d) False claim: Duress is categorically unavailable for theft — wrong because the CL bar applies only to homicide
(e) False claim: Credibility and specificity substitute for imminence — wrong because CL treats imminence as a mandatory separate element
-->

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-threat-threshold refinement; duress-imminence-proximity refinement (CL requires imminent threat); Doctrinal Framework Part II.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q4.** Returning to this jurisdiction's default rules (Fact 6), your supervisor asks about Holmes's claim that he stole the $5,000 under duress (Fact 4). Under the common law, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because Nietzsche threatened serious bodily injury to Holmes's mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's history, and the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline that places the threatened harm days in the future.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress requires a threat directed at the defendant personally, and the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members.

(c) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was not imminent -- Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, and the common law requires a present and immediate threat, not one that will materialize days later. <!-- correct -->

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law, which limits the defense to offenses against persons.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense because a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific, and Nietzsche's history of violence makes the threat both credible and specific enough to satisfy the common law standard.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the common law (Fact 6), duress requires (1) a threat of death or serious bodily injury, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) the threat targets the defendant or a close relation, and (4) no reasonable legal alternative exists. However, the threat lacks imminence under the common law's strict standard. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday -- multiple days away. This conflates the strict common-law rule with the *Dingwall* relaxation; under the common law, imminence requires a present and immediate threat, and a pattern of past violence does not substitute for temporal proximity. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, defeating the imminence element. Option (b) states that the common law "does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. Option (d) fabricates a crime-type limitation -- the false proposition is that "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes." Option (e) states that "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" -- the false proposition is this restatement of the imminence rule. The common law requires imminence as a separate element; credibility and specificity do not substitute for temporal immediacy.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence, duress-threat-threshold, duress-target-scope], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and family relationship while overlooking that "until Friday" defeats CL imminence

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-threat-threshold refinement; duress-imminence-proximity refinement (CL requires imminent threat); Doctrinal Framework Part II.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that Nietzsche’s history of violence makes the threat "operating" on Holmes's mind immediately. Under a functional view of the common law, the requirement for imminence is intended to ensure the threat is credible and leaves no time for legal alternatives. If Nietzsche's pattern of violence proves he will certainly follow through, the "deadline" of Friday is a mere formality, and the danger is effectively present from the moment of the threat. Thus, the documented pattern of carrying out threats satisfies the spirit of the imminence requirement.

(b) Argument-for: While modern interpretations of the common law often include family members, the oldest formulations of the defense were strictly limited to the person of the defendant (the "threat of death or serious bodily injury to himself"). A student might argue that the common law's default rules, if strictly applied as they existed in their most narrow historical form, do not permit vicarious duress. Therefore, because the threat was directed at Holmes's mother rather than Holmes himself, the defense fails.

(c) Argument-for: This is the standard application of the common law "present and immediate" rule. Common law duress is notoriously narrow, requiring a "gun to the head" scenario. Because Nietzsche gave Holmes a deadline of Friday—several days away—Holmes had time to seek legal alternatives, such as contacting the police. The threat was of a future harm, not a present and immediate one, which categorically disqualifies the duress defense under the common law.

(d) Argument-for: A student could argue that because duress is an excuse based on the overwhelming of the will to avoid physical harm, it is logically restricted to crimes committed against persons to prevent greater personal harm. While it is widely accepted that duress is excluded from murder, some jurisdictions or historical interpretations may have excluded property crimes because the "choice of evils" (even if duress is technically an excuse) does not favor the commission of theft when the personal threat is not immediate.

(e) Argument-for: This argument focuses on the "credible and specific" nature of the threat as the primary concern of the common law. If the threat is so specific that Holmes knows exactly what will happen and so credible that he knows it is certain to occur, the temporal proximity (imminence) becomes a secondary factor. By this logic, Nietzsche's history of violence satisfies the core concern of the common law—ensuring the defendant acted under a genuine and overwhelming fear—regardless of the temporal gap.

Head-to-head: (c) is the strongest answer because it accurately applies the rigid "present and immediate" requirement of the common law. Option (a) and (e) attempt to bypass the temporal requirement using credibility or past behavior, but the common law treats imminence as a distinct, mandatory element that is not satisfied by "inevitability." Option (b) is incorrect because the common law has long recognized "close relations" (especially a parent) as valid targets of a threat. Option (d) is incorrect because the common law limitation on duress is famously narrow, applying almost exclusively to murder, not to theft or property crimes.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline" — wrong because common law imminence is a temporal requirement (present and immediate) that cannot be substituted by a pattern of behavior.
- (b): "the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" — wrong because the common law specifically includes "close relations" (including mothers) in the scope of protected targets.
- (d): "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law" — wrong because the common law exclusion for duress applies only to homicide, not to property crimes like theft.
- (e): "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" — wrong because the common law treats imminence as a mandatory, separate element from credibility/specificity.

Recommended fix: none
-->

**Q4.** Returning to this jurisdiction's default rules (Fact 6), your supervisor asks about Holmes's claim that he stole the $5,000 under duress (Fact 4). Under the common law, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense because a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific, and Nietzsche's history of violence makes the threat both credible and specific enough to satisfy the common law standard.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the common law (Fact 6), duress requires (1) a threat of death or serious bodily injury, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) the threat targets the defendant or a close relation, and (4) no reasonable legal alternative exists. This conflates the strict common-law rule with the *Dingwall* relaxation; under the common law, imminence requires a present and immediate threat, and a pattern of past violence does not substitute for temporal proximity. <!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that option (a) is correct by drawing on specific modern exceptions or functionally relaxed standards (like battered-spouse syndrome cases) where strict temporal proximity is sometimes softened. Under this view, a documented history of violence makes a threat practically inescapable and therefore "imminent" in a functional sense. They could reason that the common law has evolved to accommodate such patterns of abuse, rendering the "Friday" deadline legally irrelevant as long as the threatener's violent history is established.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that option (b) correctly identifies an early or strict common-law limitation. Because duress is fundamentally about involuntary action driven by an immediate instinct for self-preservation, one might incorrectly conclude that threats to third parties, even family members, cannot legally overbear a defendant's will to excuse a crime. This relies on an overly rigid, archaic view of the defense's target scope.
(c) Argument-for: Option (c) relies on the orthodox common-law definition of duress, which strictly requires the threat to be present, imminent, and impending. Because Nietzsche's threat gave Holmes until Friday—multiple days away—the danger was not immediate. Consequently, Holmes had time to seek legal alternatives, meaning his duress defense fails under the traditional strict imminence standard.
(d) Argument-for: A student might select option (d) by conflating the common-law bar on using duress for murder with a broader prohibition on its use for specific property crimes. They might rationalize that crimes involving calculated theft, unlike defensive force or necessity-driven acts against nature, categorically cannot be justified by external threats under the common law. This incorrectly assumes the common law applies categorical offense-type exclusions beyond homicide.
(e) Argument-for: Option (e) appeals to a student who confuses the strict common-law imminence requirement with more flexible modern approaches, such as the Model Penal Code. Under this reasoning, the ultimate test is whether the threat was credible and specific enough to overbear the will of a reasonable person. If credibility and specificity are established, the strict temporal requirement of imminence is arguably functionally satisfied or legally waived.

Head-to-head: Option (c) is the only legally accurate application of the common-law duress standard, correctly recognizing that a threat materializing days in the future fails the strict imminence requirement. The distractors all employ absolute, falsifiable language to explicitly misstate common-law doctrine. Options (a) and (e) confidently state false legal rules about imminence, claiming it is automatically satisfied by past patterns or credibility alone. Option (b) makes a clear, false claim about the target scope (excluding family members), and option (d) fabricates a categorical ban on duress for theft. The distractors are appropriately locked and unambiguously false.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline" — wrong because common law strict imminence requires a present and immediate threat; past patterns do not override future deadlines.
- (b): "the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" — wrong because the common law recognizes threats against close relations, such as a mother.
- (d): "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law" — wrong because the categorical exclusion at common law applies to homicide, not theft.
- (e): "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" — wrong because the common law treats imminence as a mandatory, independent temporal requirement.

Recommended fix: none

---

## Q05

### Grounding

**Q5.** Assume, counterfactually, that this case arose in a jurisdiction that has adopted the *Dingwall* reasoning on imminence in duress cases. Assume further that Nietzsche had explicitly commanded Holmes to steal the money from the shelter, that Nietzsche had been threatening Holmes for months, and that Holmes reasonably believed local police were unable to protect his mother. Does the *Dingwall* approach allow Holmes to satisfy the imminence requirement for duress? (Fact 4, modified.)

(a) Yes, because *Dingwall* established that any pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats creates a reasonable perception of imminent danger, regardless of the relationship between the coercer and the defendant.
(b) No, because the *Dingwall* reasoning relaxing the strict physical imminence requirement is specifically tied to the dynamics of battering and coercive control, which do not apply to a generalized extortion dispute between former associates. <!-- correct -->
(c) Yes, because *Dingwall* eliminates the imminence requirement entirely, allowing any credible threat of future harm to satisfy the defense if the threatening party has a track record of violence.
(d) No, because the *Dingwall* exception applies only when the defendant is physically confined or restrained by the threatening party at the exact moment the crime is committed.
(e) No, because the *Dingwall* exception is strictly limited to cases where the threatened harm is directed at the defendant personally, and cannot be invoked based on threats to a third party.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under *United States v. Dingwall*, the strict physical proximity and imminence requirements of duress are relaxed for defendants experiencing the effects of battering or coercive control. The specific psychological dynamics of battering can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger even when the coercer is physically absent. However, courts do not extend this exception to generalized criminal extortion or associate disputes, as doing so would swallow the traditional strict imminence rule. Thus, (b) is correct. Option (a) falsely broadens *Dingwall* into a blanket exception for any sustained threats. Option (c) falsely asserts *Dingwall* eliminates the imminence requirement entirely. Option (d) falsely claims physical confinement is required; *Dingwall* expressly held the batterer's physical presence or confinement is not necessary. Option (e) falsely states that duress under *Dingwall* cannot be based on threats to third parties; traditional duress principles covering threats to close relatives still apply.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students incorrectly apply Dingwall's relaxed imminence to general extortion cases rather than limiting it to battering/coercive control

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duress imminence, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-imminence-proximity`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Edge Case Audit

Option (e) falsely states that duress under *Dingwall* cannot be based on threats to third parties; traditional duress principles covering threats to close relatives still apply.

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.
