### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code. <!-- correct -->
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation rather than on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement for the threshold element.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, and mere verbal threats of a beating cannot qualify as serious bodily injury.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats do not satisfy the "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person would have reported the threats to police before March 15, rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option accurately summarizes the standard definitions of duress. Under common law, the threat of a beating requiring "surgical repair" meets the threshold of "serious bodily injury," and a mother qualifies as a "close relation." Under MPC § 2.09, the standard is even lower ("unlawful force") and the target scope is broader ("any person"), making the defense applicable under both standards.

(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that traditional common law was extremely narrow to prevent fraudulent defenses and collusion. While modern courts have expanded the scope, some older or stricter jurisdictions historically limited the defense to threats against the defendant's own life or limb. This argument posits that the common law target scope remains strictly personal, excluding even close family members like a mother.

(c) Argument-for: This argument focuses on the five-day gap between the threat (March 10) and the crime (March 15). Although the MPC text does not use the word "imminent," a student might argue that the "person of reasonable firmness" standard effectively incorporates an imminence requirement. If the threat is for future harm, a person of reasonable firmness would seek police protection rather than committing the crime, thus the MPC functionally retains the common law imminence threshold.

(d) Argument-for: This argument hinges on a strict interpretation of "serious bodily injury." Under a high-threshold common law standard, only threats of death or permanent, life-threatening disfigurement count. One could argue that a "week in the hospital" for a beating is a significant but non-deadly battery that falls short of the "deadly force" or "grievous harm" required by the most rigorous common law formulations, whereas the MPC's "unlawful force" is much easier to satisfy.

(e) Argument-for: This option emphasizes the affirmative duty to avoid the crime when possible. If the defendant had five days to contact authorities, a court might rule that the threat is insufficient as a matter of law because the "reasonable firmness" test (MPC) and the "no reasonable opportunity to escape" test (CL) both require the absence of a safe legal alternative. Failing to report the threat when there is ample time renders the defense unavailable.

Head-to-head: (a) is the strongest answer because it accurately identifies the core expansions and definitions of both the common law and the MPC. (b) is weak because the common law has long recognized "close relatives" in the target scope. (c) is legally incorrect because MPC § 2.09 explicitly abandoned the "imminence" requirement that existed at common law. (d) is incorrect because "serious bodily injury" is a well-established alternative to "deadly force" at common law. (e) is incorrect because it mislabels the "reasonable firmness" test as a common-law standard and wrongly treats a factual question of "opportunity to escape" as an automatic threshold bar for the "threat" element itself.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally." — wrong because common law target scope extends to close relations (parents, spouses, children).
- (c): "Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 purposefully omits imminence, replacing it with the "person of reasonable firmness" test.
- (d): "common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force" — wrong because threats of serious bodily injury (not just death) satisfy the common law threshold.
- (e): "fails under both standards because... 'reasonable firmness' test" — wrong because "reasonable firmness" is an MPC-specific term; the common law uses an objective standard focused on "reasonable opportunity to escape."

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code. <!-- correct -->
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation rather than on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement for the threshold element.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, and mere verbal threats of a beating cannot qualify as serious bodily injury.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats do not satisfy the "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person would have reported the threats to police before March 15, rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option correctly maps the facts to the doctrinal elements. Under the common law, duress requires a threat of death or serious bodily injury (which a week-long hospital stay clearly satisfies) directed at the defendant or a close relation (which a mother satisfies). The Model Penal Code broadens these elements to "unlawful force" against "another," which is also plainly satisfied here. Thus, the threat threshold and target scope are met under both tests.
(b) Argument-for: A student might recall that common-law duress is highly restrictive compared to the Model Penal Code. By strictly construing the defense, a student could incorrectly deduce that traditional common law requires the threat to be directed solely at the defendant, making the threat against the mother legally immaterial for target scope purposes under the common law.
(c) Argument-for: Imminence is widely known as the hallmark of a common-law duress defense. Because Bentham's threat occurred five days before the robbery, a student could argue it constitutes a threat of future harm. Assuming that the Model Penal Code, while broader in target scope, maintains basic temporal proximity to prevent vigilantism or unnecessary crimes, a student could conclude that the MPC retains strict imminence as part of its threshold requirement.
(d) Argument-for: The common law sets an extremely high bar for duress. A student might interpret the common-law requirement of "death or grievous bodily harm" to practically mean an immediate, lethal threat—like a gun to the head. Under this strict (albeit exaggerated) view, Bentham's verbal threat of a future beating by a third party would fail the common-law threshold entirely, even while passing the MPC's looser "unlawful force" standard.
(e) Argument-for: Both the common law and the Model Penal Code require that the defendant's response meet an objective standard. With a five-day gap between the threat and the crime, an ordinary person of reasonable firmness would have sought police protection. A student could argue that this extended delay and failure to escape defeats the threat threshold as a matter of law, rendering the defense unavailable before it ever reaches a jury.

Head-to-head: Option (a) is correct because it accurately applies the elements of duress for both the common law and MPC to the facts. Every distractor employs an explicit false legal claim to justify its conclusion. Option (b) explicitly denies the "close relation" exception in common law. Option (c) explicitly claims the MPC retains the strict imminence requirement. Option (d) explicitly fabricates an "immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force" requirement for the common law. Option (e) conflates the failure to report/escape with the threshold element and falsely classifies the MPC's reasonable firmness test as failing "as a matter of law" in this scenario, rather than being a jury question.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law expressly recognizes threats against "close relations" (such as a mother).
- (c): "the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement for the threshold element" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 explicitly drops the strict imminence requirement, replacing it with the broader "reasonable firmness" standard.
- (d): "the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force" — wrong because the common law also recognizes threats of serious or grievous bodily injury, not just deadly force.
- (e): "rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law" — wrong because under the common law, failure to report goes to the "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element rather than the threat threshold, and under the MPC, whether a person of reasonable firmness would have been coerced despite the delay is a factual question for the jury.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q13

### Grounding

**Q13.** Assume that Kant, threatened by Bentham with serious bodily injury to Kant's mother, satisfies the elements of duress for the predicate armed robbery, during which his only role was driving the getaway van. Assume further that Bentham (not Kant) fired the fatal shot that killed Bayes. How does the defense of duress apply to Kant for felony murder under the common law, and for murder under the Model Penal Code?

(a) Duress is universally available for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because the defense automatically applies whenever a defendant faces a threat of serious bodily injury to a relative.
(b) Duress is categorically unavailable for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because both legal frameworks enforce an absolute bar against asserting duress for any homicide offense.
(c) Under the common law *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception, duress is available for felony murder only if the defendant was unaware their co-felon was armed; under the Model Penal Code, duress is categorically barred for murder.
(d) Under the traditional common law, duress is barred for felony murder even for a non-killer participant, but Kant may successfully assert necessity because avoiding injury to his mother is legally weighed as a greater good than Bayes's death.
(e) Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to felony murder, but the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for a non-killer participant who satisfies the defense for the predicate felony. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available because § 2.09 contains no homicide exception. <!-- correct -->

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) is correct. Under the traditional common law, the duress defense is barred for all forms of murder, including felony murder. However, the modern *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for felony murder if the defendant is a non-killer participant whose involvement in the predicate felony was excused by duress. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available for murder because § 2.09 lacks a categorical homicide exception. (a) is incorrect because the traditional common law bars duress for murder. (b) is incorrect because the MPC allows duress as a defense to homicide. (c) is incorrect because the *Reichard* exception does not require ignorance of a co-felon's weapon, and the MPC does not bar duress for murder. (d) is incorrect because necessity (choice of evils) typically excludes homicide at common law, and avoiding physical injury is not legally recognized as a greater good than preserving a human life.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, murder-bar, felony-murder-duress-exception, cross-doctrine], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (CL murder bar; Model Penal Code § 2.09; People v. Reichard, 505 Mich. 81 (2020); McMillan v. State, 181 Md. App. 298 (2008)).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duress murder bar and felony murder exception, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-murder-bar` and `felony-murder-duress-exception`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q13.** Assume that Kant, threatened by Bentham with serious bodily injury to Kant's mother, satisfies the elements of duress for the predicate armed robbery, during which his only role was driving the getaway van. Assume further that Bentham (not Kant) fired the fatal shot that killed Bayes. How does the defense of duress apply to Kant for felony murder under the common law, and for murder under the Model Penal Code?

(a) Duress is universally available for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because the defense automatically applies whenever a defendant faces a threat of serious bodily injury to a relative.
(b) Duress is categorically unavailable for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because both legal frameworks enforce an absolute bar against asserting duress for any homicide offense.
(c) Under the common law *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception, duress is available for felony murder only if the defendant was unaware their co-felon was armed; under the Model Penal Code, duress is categorically barred for murder.
(d) Under the traditional common law, duress is barred for felony murder even for a non-killer participant, but Kant may successfully assert necessity because avoiding injury to his mother is legally weighed as a greater good than Bayes's death.
(e) Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to felony murder, but the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for a non-killer participant who satisfies the defense for the predicate felony. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available because § 2.09 contains no homicide exception.

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) is correct. Under the traditional common law, the duress defense is barred for all forms of murder, including felony murder. However, the modern *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for felony murder if the defendant is a non-killer participant whose involvement in the predicate felony was excused by duress. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available for murder because § 2.09 lacks a categorical homicide exception. (a) is incorrect because the traditional common law bars duress for murder. (b) is incorrect because the MPC allows duress as a defense to homicide. (c) is incorrect because the *Reichard* exception does not require ignorance of a co-felon's weapon, and the MPC does not bar duress for murder. (d) is incorrect because necessity (choice of evils) typically excludes homicide at common law, and avoiding physical injury is not legally recognized as a greater good than preserving a human life.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, murder-bar, felony-murder-duress-exception, cross-doctrine], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (CL murder bar; Model Penal Code § 2.09; People v. Reichard, 505 Mich. 81 (2020); McMillan v. State, 181 Md. App. 298 (2008)).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies that under the modern trend and the MPC, duress is not conceptually barred from homicide. In the context of felony murder, if the predicate felony (robbery) is excused by duress, the "felony" element of felony murder is extinguished, making the defense "universally available" to non-killer accomplices across modern interpretations. Furthermore, both CL and MPC recognize that threats to close family members like a mother are legally sufficient to trigger the defense, effectively making the application automatic once the threat to a relative is proven and the "reasonable person" standard is met.

(b) Argument-for: This argument rests on the traditional "murder bar," which states that one must die oneself rather than kill an innocent person. At common law, this bar was absolute and extended to all forms of homicide, including felony murder, because the law refused to balance lives. Under the MPC, while § 2.09 does not explicitly exclude murder, many scholars argue that a person of "reasonable firmness" would categorically refuse to participate in a lethal crime, effectively creating an absolute bar in practice that aligns with the common law's moral stance.

(c) Argument-for: The *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception is designed to protect those with low culpability; thus, it is a reasonable legal interpretation that the exception "only" applies if the defendant was unaware of the lethal potential (the weapon) of the enterprise. If the defendant knew of the gun, they assumed the risk of the homicide, forfeiting the exception. Simultaneously, several jurisdictions adopting MPC-like codes have legislatively re-inserted the homicide bar, making it plausible to argue that under a "Model" framework, murder remains categorically excluded.

(d) Argument-for: Traditional common law strictly applied the duress murder bar to felony murder, treating the getaway driver as a principal in the first degree through the felony murder rule. However, when duress (an excuse) is barred, a defendant might turn to necessity (a justification). This argument posits that saving a mother's life (or preventing her serious injury) can be legally framed as a "choice of evils" where the defendant's non-lethal participation in a robbery is a lesser evil than the bodily mutilation of a family member.

(e) Argument-for: This option accurately synthesizes the evolution of the law. It acknowledges the traditional common law rule (the murder bar) while correctly identifying the specific modern exception created by cases like *People v. Reichard* and *McMillan v. State*, which allow duress for felony murder if the defendant did not personally kill. It also correctly states that the MPC’s duress provision (§ 2.09) is distinctive because it lacks the categorical homicide exception found in traditional common law.

Head-to-head: Option (e) is the strongest because it correctly identifies the specific names of the cases (*Reichard/McMillan*) that carve out the felony murder exception, whereas (c) adds a fictional "unaware of weapon" requirement. Option (a) fails because duress is not "universally" available (the traditional bar still exists in many states) and it is never "automatic" (it always requires the reasonable person/firmness test). Option (b) is objectively false regarding the MPC's text. Option (d) fails because the law of necessity (choice of evils) almost never permits a trade-off of a victim's life for a relative's bodily injury, and the MPC usually treats necessity and duress as distinct doctrines with necessity being even more restrictive regarding homicide.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "because the defense automatically applies" — wrong because duress always requires a factual determination of whether a person of reasonable firmness would have succumbed; it is never automatic.
- (b): "both legal frameworks enforce an absolute bar" — wrong because the MPC § 2.09 does not contain a homicide bar.
- (c): "only if the defendant was unaware their co-felon was armed" — wrong because the *Reichard/McMillan* exception hinges on whether the defendant was a "non-killer," not on their specific knowledge of the weapon.
- (d): "avoiding injury to his mother is legally weighed as a greater good than Bayes's death" — wrong because under the "choice of evils" (necessity) doctrine, preserving life is almost always legally superior to avoiding bodily injury.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q13.** Assume that Kant, threatened by Bentham with serious bodily injury to Kant's mother, satisfies the elements of duress for the predicate armed robbery, during which his only role was driving the getaway van. Assume further that Bentham (not Kant) fired the fatal shot that killed Bayes. How does the defense of duress apply to Kant for felony murder under the common law, and for murder under the Model Penal Code?

(a) Duress is universally available for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because the defense automatically applies whenever a defendant faces a threat of serious bodily injury to a relative.
(b) Duress is categorically unavailable for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because both legal frameworks enforce an absolute bar against asserting duress for any homicide offense.
(c) Under the common law *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception, duress is available for felony murder only if the defendant was unaware their co-felon was armed; under the Model Penal Code, duress is categorically barred for murder.
(d) Under the traditional common law, duress is barred for felony murder even for a non-killer participant, but Kant may successfully assert necessity because avoiding injury to his mother is legally weighed as a greater good than Bayes's death.
(e) Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to felony murder, but the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for a non-killer participant who satisfies the defense for the predicate felony. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available because § 2.09 contains no homicide exception. <!-- correct -->

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) is correct. Under the traditional common law, the duress defense is barred for all forms of murder, including felony murder. However, the modern *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for felony murder if the defendant is a non-killer participant whose involvement in the predicate felony was excused by duress. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available for murder because § 2.09 lacks a categorical homicide exception. (a) is incorrect because the traditional common law bars duress for murder. (b) is incorrect because the MPC allows duress as a defense to homicide. (c) is incorrect because the *Reichard* exception does not require ignorance of a co-felon's weapon, and the MPC does not bar duress for murder. (d) is incorrect because necessity (choice of evils) typically excludes homicide at common law, and avoiding physical injury is not legally recognized as a greater good than preserving a human life.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, murder-bar, felony-murder-duress-exception, cross-doctrine], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (CL murder bar; Model Penal Code § 2.09; People v. Reichard, 505 Mich. 81 (2020); McMillan v. State, 181 Md. App. 298 (2008)).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under the MPC, duress is broadly available, and a student might mistakenly believe the modern common law matches this broad availability. The student could reason that because the threat was to a close relative (Kant's mother) and involved serious bodily injury, the elements of duress are so thoroughly satisfied that the defense overrides any homicide exclusions, making it "universally available."
(b) Argument-for: The traditional common law enforces a well-known, strict categorical bar on applying duress to any form of murder, requiring a person to sacrifice themselves rather than take an innocent life. A student could plausibly assume that the Model Penal Code, known for strict liability elements in some homicide areas, adopted this same historic, absolute moral bar against duress for murder.
(c) Argument-for: The *Reichard* and *McMillan* cases carved out exceptions for non-killer accomplices in felony murder. A student could logically infer that to avoid culpability for the homicide, the non-killer must lack foreseeability of the death, requiring them to be unaware the co-felon was armed. Furthermore, they might falsely assume the MPC categorically bars duress for murder as a standard choice-of-evils limitation.
(d) Argument-for: Under traditional common law, the murder bar strictly prevents duress from being applied to felony murder. A student might distinguish duress from necessity (choice of evils) and mistakenly argue that, under a utilitarian calculation, saving one's mother from "serious bodily injury" could be argued to a jury as a greater legal good than the life of the victim, especially if the victim's death was somewhat accidental or the relative's injury was certain.
(e) Argument-for: Traditional common law definitively bars duress for all murder, including felony murder. However, modern jurisprudence (*Reichard*/*McMillan*) established an exception allowing non-killer participants to use duress as a defense to felony murder if they were coerced into the underlying predicate felony. Moreover, Model Penal Code § 2.09 explicitly rejects the traditional common law murder bar, allowing duress for any crime, including murder, provided the defendant meets the "person of reasonable firmness" standard.

Head-to-head: 
Option (e) correctly integrates the traditional common law rule, the modern *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception, and the Model Penal Code's lack of a homicide bar. Option (a) uses absolute language ("universally available," "automatically applies") that wrongly ignores the traditional common law murder bar and the specific elements required to prove duress. Option (b) falsely claims the MPC enforces an absolute bar on duress for homicide, directly contradicting § 2.09. Option (c) relies on a fabricated requirement (unawareness of the weapon) for the *Reichard* exception and incorrectly asserts the MPC bars duress for murder. Option (d) incorrectly applies the necessity defense, as the common law never legally weighs avoiding serious bodily injury as a greater good than innocent human life. All distractors contain explicitly locked false legal claims.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "Duress is universally available for felony murder under the common law" and "automatically applies whenever" — wrong because traditional common law explicitly bars duress for murder, and the defense never "automatically" applies without meeting all specific elements (e.g., reasonable firmness).
- (b): "both legal frameworks enforce an absolute bar against asserting duress for any homicide offense" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 deliberately omits the common law homicide exception.
- (c): "under the Model Penal Code, duress is categorically barred for murder" — wrong because the MPC expressly permits duress for murder.
- (d): "avoiding injury to his mother is legally weighed as a greater good than Bayes's death" — wrong because under the traditional common law choice of evils/necessity defense, preserving an innocent human life is always legally superior to (or at least equal to) avoiding physical injury.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q14

### Grounding

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at Kant's chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. Goetz*, evaluate whether Kant's belief that deadly force was immediately necessary was reasonable.

(a) Kant's belief was reasonable because *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation, which asks only whether the defendant himself regarded his response as warranted.

(b) A reasonable person in Kant's situation — facing Bentham leveling a revolver at his chest from six feet, with the only exit fifteen feet away without cover, and with knowledge of Bentham's explicit threat of hospitalizing violence — could reasonably believe deadly force was immediately necessary; *Goetz* permits the factfinder to incorporate the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker in the objective inquiry. <!-- correct -->

(c) Kant's belief was unreasonable because the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker or the attacker's reputation for violence; those are subjective elements that would convert the reasonable-person test into a defendant-specific inquiry.

(d) Kant's belief was reasonable because New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation, which asks only whether the defendant believed deadly force was necessary, without requiring objective reasonableness.

(e) Kant's belief was reasonable because the controlling inquiry is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist firing, and Bentham's leveled revolver and explicit threat plainly overcome that threshold.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly applies *Goetz*, which held that reasonableness incorporates the defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and specific knowledge of the attacker — not a purely external, context-stripped inquiry. The leveled revolver from six feet, the absence of any safe exit, and Bentham's prior explicit threat of hospitalizing violence all inform what a reasonable person in Kant's situation would have perceived. (a) falsely attributes the "reasonable to him" formulation to *Goetz*; the Court of Appeals expressly rejected that Appellate Division reading and retained an objective element. (c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — objective reasonableness, Ch 22, Refinement `objective-reasonableness-standard` and `situational-incorporation`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at his chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. **Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Although the Court of Appeals in *Goetz* eventually rejected the Appellate Division's purely subjective test, the core of the defense's argument—and the initial lower court ruling—was that a defendant's actions must be judged from their own perspective to be fair. An advocate could argue that by incorporating the defendant's "situation" and "prior experiences," *Goetz* effectively allows a jury to decide if the act was "reasonable to him." This ensures that the jury accounts for the specific physical and mental state of the defendant at the time of the shooting.

(b) Argument-for: Under *People v. Goetz*, the New York standard for reasonableness is an objective one that nonetheless accounts for the defendant's specific "circumstances" and "situation." This includes any prior knowledge the defendant had of the attacker, such as Bentham's specific threat to hospitalize Kant. Given the immediate physical threat of a leveled firearm and the lack of accessible cover or a nearby exit, a reasonable person possessing Kant's knowledge would logically conclude that deadly force was necessary to prevent his own death or serious injury.

(c) Argument-for: One could argue that to remain truly "objective," the *Goetz* standard must look at the immediate physical facts of the encounter—the six-foot distance and the revolver—rather than the history between the parties. Allowing a jury to consider prior threats or reputations for violence risks turning the trial into a character assassination of the victim rather than a clinical evaluation of the immediate threat. From this perspective, the "reasonable person" is an abstract construct that reacts to the barrel of a gun, not to personal grievances or prior non-lethal threats like a "hospitalization" comment.

(d) Argument-for: An advocate might argue that the inclusion of the word "reasonably" in the New York statute is a mere formal hurdle, and that in practice, New York aligns with the Model Penal Code's emphasis on the defendant's internal belief. Since the law requires the jury to step into the defendant's shoes and look through his eyes, the inquiry is fundamentally subjective. If Kant truly believed he was in danger of death, his belief should satisfy the standard regardless of how an external "reasonable person" might feel.

(e) Argument-for: In many areas of criminal law, such as duress, the "person of reasonable firmness" is the standard for evaluating how much pressure a defendant is expected to withstand. A student might argue that self-defense is essentially a subset of this logic: would a person of reasonable firmness, facing a loaded gun and a prior threat of violence, feel compelled to fire? This provides a clear, high-threshold benchmark that matches the intensity of a lethal encounter like the one between Bentham and Kant.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the strongest because it perfectly captures the "hybrid" nature of the *Goetz* standard—retaining the objective "reasonable person" while populating that person with the defendant's specific situational knowledge. Options (a), (c), and (d) each take one side of the subjective/objective divide that *Goetz* explicitly attempted to bridge, making them legally inaccurate. Option (e) is a "category error" distractor, importing the standard for duress into a self-defense analysis.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "*Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's 'reasonable to him' formulation" — wrong because *Goetz* expressly rejected this formulation in favor of an objective standard.
- (c): "*Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences" — wrong because *Goetz* explicitly held that the "situation" include the defendant's relevant prior experiences.
- (d): "New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation" — wrong because *Goetz* specifically noted that the NY legislature rejected the MPC approach by requiring that the defendant "reasonably" believe force is necessary.
- (e): "controlling inquiry is whether a 'person of reasonable firmness' would have been unable to resist firing" — wrong because "reasonable firmness" is the standard for duress under MPC § 2.09, not self-defense under NYPL § 35.15.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at Kant's chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. (c) Kant's belief was unreasonable because the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker or the attacker's reputation for violence; those are subjective elements that would convert the reasonable-person test into a defendant-specific inquiry.

(d) Kant's belief was reasonable because New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation, which asks only whether the defendant believed deadly force was necessary, without requiring objective reasonableness.

(e) Kant's belief was reasonable because the controlling inquiry is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist firing, and Bentham's leveled revolver and explicit threat plainly overcome that threshold.

(a) falsely attributes the "reasonable to him" formulation to *Goetz*; the Court of Appeals expressly rejected that Appellate Division reading and retained an objective element. (c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that *Goetz* emphasized evaluating the circumstances from the defendant's perspective. The Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation captured the reality that the factfinder must step into the defendant's shoes. Because the Court of Appeals required the jury to consider the defendant's specific background, physical attributes, and prior experiences, a student might conclude that the highest court essentially adopted the "reasonable to him" standard in substance, even if it refined the nomenclature.
(b) Argument-for: A student could point out that *Goetz* famously established a hybrid objective-subjective standard for self-defense in New York. The Court held that while the belief must be objectively reasonable, the "reasonable person" must be placed in the defendant's exact "situation." This situation explicitly includes the physical realities of the encounter (a leveled gun, no cover) as well as the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker's prior threats. Thus, a reasonable person in Kant's exact situation could find deadly force immediately necessary.
(c) Argument-for: A student might argue that *Goetz* explicitly rejected the Model Penal Code's subjective approach in favor of retaining an objective standard. To maintain a true objective standard, the inquiry must be cleansed of the defendant's subjective biases, fears, and idiosyncratic prior experiences. If the jury considers the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker, it risks converting the test back into the rejected subjective "reasonable to him" standard. Therefore, true objective reasonableness must exclude these subjective historical factors.
(d) Argument-for: A student might recall that the New York penal code revisions in the 1960s were heavily influenced by the Model Penal Code. The MPC deliberately abandoned the strict objective standard for self-defense, requiring only that the actor genuinely believe the force was necessary. A student could mistakenly believe that New York adopted this MPC provision wholesale, meaning Kant's honest, subjective belief is enough to satisfy the statute without an objective reasonableness requirement.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that when an actor is facing an immediate, terrifying threat like a leveled revolver, the situation triggers the "person of reasonable firmness" standard. This standard, widely used in necessity and duress contexts to evaluate objectively reasonable responses to overwhelming pressure, perfectly captures the terror of a gun pointed at one's chest. Thus, the controlling objective test is whether a reasonably firm person would succumb to the pressure and fire.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the clear correct answer, as it accurately applies the *Goetz* standard, which incorporates the defendant's "situation" (including prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker) into the objective reasonable person test. Option (a) fails because it explicitly states *Goetz* "adopted" the Appellate Division's standard, a historically and legally false claim since the Court of Appeals reversed the Appellate Division specifically to reject that formulation. Option (c) fails because it categorically asserts the standard "excludes any reference" to prior experiences, directly contradicting *Goetz*'s holding that the defendant's situation encompasses such experiences. Option (d) fails because it falsely claims New York adopted the "purely subjective formulation" of the MPC without requiring objective reasonableness, ignoring the legislature's addition of the word "reasonably." Option (e) fails because it incorrectly identifies the "person of reasonable firmness" test—which belongs to duress—as the controlling inquiry for self-defense. All distractors feature clear, falsifiable legal errors.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "*Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's 'reasonable to him' formulation" — wrong because the Court of Appeals expressly rejected the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" standard to preserve an objective requirement.
- (c): "excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker" — wrong because *Goetz* explicitly held that the objective standard must incorporate the physical circumstances and the defendant's prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker.
- (d): "New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation" — wrong because the New York legislature deliberately added the word "reasonably" to the MPC's language, requiring objective reasonableness.
- (e): "the controlling inquiry is whether a 'person of reasonable firmness' would have been unable to resist" — wrong because this formulation states the objective standard for the excuse of duress (MPC § 2.09), not the justification of self-defense.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q15

### Grounding

**Q15.** Based on the standoff at the warehouse, assume a jury finds Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity" despite any potential defenses. Evaluate how the duty-to-retreat analysis would proceed across three jurisdictions: New York, Florida, and California.

(a) Kant is entitled to Stand Your Ground immunity in Florida regardless of the ongoing proceeds transfer, because the statute's "engaged in criminal activity" limitation applies only to activity unrelated to the immediate confrontation; Bentham's aggression severs any connection to the predicate robbery.

(b) The New York duty to retreat is not satisfied unless a reasonably prudent person would have retreated using any available escape route, regardless of safety; the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes an available route as a matter of law.

(c) In California, Kant has a duty to retreat because California applies the traditional retreat rule to all non-dwelling confrontations, and the warehouse is a commercial space.

(d) In New York, the duty to retreat applies only if retreat is available "with complete personal safety," and a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver likely does not qualify as a safe retreat; in Florida, Kant loses the statutory right to stand his ground because the statute excludes those "engaged in criminal activity," meaning he reverts to a duty to retreat; in California, there is no duty to retreat. <!-- correct -->

(e) The castle doctrine applies to the warehouse in all three jurisdictions because Kant was a business invitee, and all three recognize a workplace extension of the castle doctrine.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly traces each jurisdiction. NY requires retreat only if available "with complete personal safety"; a fifteen-foot traverse of open floor against a leveled revolver from six feet likely fails that standard. Florida's stand-your-ground statute revokes the right to stand one's ground (reimposing a duty to retreat) when the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity" (Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2)), which the prompt stipulates applies here. CA imposes no duty to retreat by case law. (a) fabricates a "severance" doctrine; FL's statutory limitation is categorical. (b) misstates the NY standard; NY requires retreat only with "complete personal safety." (c) misstates CA law; CA imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. (e) fabricates a universal workplace castle-doctrine rule; none of the three treat a warehouse where the defendant is a criminal participant as a "castle."

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (NY "complete safety" retreat rule; Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2) SYG "criminal activity" bar; CA no-duty-to-retreat rule; castle-doctrine scope).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Duty to Retreat and Stand Your Ground, Ch 22, Refinement `duty-to-retreat-general`, `stand-your-ground-statute`, and `castle-doctrine-scope`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity" despite any potential defenses. (c) In California, Kant has a duty to retreat because California applies the traditional retreat rule to all non-dwelling confrontations, and the warehouse is a commercial space.

(d) In New York, the duty to retreat applies only if retreat is available "with complete personal safety," and a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver likely does not qualify as a safe retreat; in Florida, Kant loses the statutory right to stand his ground because the statute excludes those "engaged in criminal activity," meaning he reverts to a duty to retreat; in California, there is no duty to retreat. <!-- correct -->

(b) misstates the NY standard; NY requires retreat only with "complete personal safety." (c) misstates CA law; CA imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. (e) fabricates a universal workplace castle-doctrine rule; none of the three treat a warehouse where the defendant is a criminal participant as a "castle."

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q15.** Based on the standoff at the warehouse, assume a jury finds Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity" despite any potential defenses. Evaluate how the duty-to-retreat analysis would proceed across three jurisdictions: New York, Florida, and California.

(a) Kant is entitled to Stand Your Ground immunity in Florida regardless of the ongoing proceeds transfer, because the statute's "engaged in criminal activity" limitation applies only to activity unrelated to the immediate confrontation; Bentham's aggression severs any connection to the predicate robbery.

(b) The New York duty to retreat is not satisfied unless a reasonably prudent person would have retreated using any available escape route, regardless of safety; the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes an available route as a matter of law.

(c) In California, Kant has a duty to retreat because California applies the traditional retreat rule to all non-dwelling confrontations, and the warehouse is a commercial space.

(d) In New York, the duty to retreat applies only if retreat is available "with complete personal safety," and a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver likely does not qualify as a safe retreat; in Florida, Kant loses the statutory right to stand his ground because the statute excludes those "engaged in criminal activity," meaning he reverts to a duty to retreat; in California, there is no duty to retreat. <!-- correct -->

(e) The castle doctrine applies to the warehouse in all three jurisdictions because Kant was a business invitee, and all three recognize a workplace extension of the castle doctrine.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly traces each jurisdiction. NY requires retreat only if available "with complete personal safety"; a fifteen-foot traverse of open floor against a leveled revolver from six feet likely fails that standard. Florida's stand-your-ground statute revokes the right to stand one's ground (reimposing a duty to retreat) when the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity" (Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2)), which the prompt stipulates applies here. CA imposes no duty to retreat by case law. (a) fabricates a "severance" doctrine; FL's statutory limitation is categorical. (b) misstates the NY standard; NY requires retreat only with "complete personal safety." (c) misstates CA law; CA imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. (e) fabricates a universal workplace castle-doctrine rule; none of the three treat a warehouse where the defendant is a criminal participant as a "castle."

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (NY "complete safety" retreat rule; Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2) SYG "criminal activity" bar; CA no-duty-to-retreat rule; castle-doctrine scope).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under Florida law, the "engaged in criminal activity" bar to Stand Your Ground immunity requires a causal nexus between the crime and the confrontation. A student could argue that if the "criminal activity" (receiving proceeds) is merely the background setting and Bentham’s decision to pull a gun was an independent, unforeseeable act of aggression, the criminal activity is legally severed from the self-defense claim. In this view, Kant is not "engaged" in the activity for the purpose of the self-defense analysis once the robbery has concluded and a new, distinct threat emerges. Therefore, he would retain the statutory right to stand his ground.

(b) Argument-for: New York’s duty to retreat is a rigorous objective standard designed to favor the preservation of life. A student could argue that "complete personal safety" is interpreted through the lens of a reasonably prudent person, and a fifteen-foot distance to an exit represents a clear, unobstructed path. By leveling the revolver from six feet away, Bentham has created an exigency where any hesitation is fatal, but the proximity of the exit makes the retreat "available" as a matter of law. Under this interpretation, the law demands that a defendant attempt the exit if it is physically present and unobstructed.

(c) Argument-for: While many perceive California as a "stand your ground" state, this is a matter of jury instructions rather than statutory decree. A student could argue that California follows the traditional common law retreat rule for all confrontations occurring outside the home (the "dwelling"). Since a warehouse is a commercial space and not a residence, the traditional common law requirement—that one must exhaust all avenues of retreat before resorting to deadly force—would apply to Kant.

(d) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the specific legal standards in all three states. New York Penal Law § 35.15(2)(a) explicitly conditions the duty to retreat on the ability to do so with "complete personal safety," which is unlikely when facing a leveled firearm at close range. Florida Statute § 776.012(2) provides that the right to stand one's ground is available only to a person "not engaged in a criminal activity." Finally, California is a "true man" jurisdiction by judicial precedent (e.g., People v. Estrada), holding there is no duty to retreat regardless of the location of the encounter.

(e) Argument-for: The "Business Castle Doctrine" is a recognized extension of the traditional home-based rule in many jurisdictions. A student could argue that because Kant was at the warehouse for a business transaction (even if illegal), he attained the status of a business invitee. If a jurisdiction treats the workplace as a "castle"—a place where one has a right to be and no duty to flee—then Kant would have no duty to retreat in New York, Florida, or California, as all three recognize some form of protection for lawful (or seemingly lawful) occupants of a premises.

Head-to-head: Option (d) is the only selection that accurately navigates the statutory and common-law nuances of all three states. (a) is weak because Florida's "criminal activity" bar is generally treated as a bright-line disqualifier for statutory immunity. (b) contains a fatal legal error by claiming NY requires retreat "regardless of safety," which contradicts the express "complete personal safety" language of NY Penal Law. (c) is incorrect because California is famously a no-duty-to-retreat state in all settings. (e) fails because the castle doctrine does not categorically apply to commercial warehouses in these states, especially for those currently engaged in criminal activity. Option (d) is the strongest because it correctly identifies that California's no-duty rule is a matter of common law/jury instructions and Florida's statutory rule has a specific criminal-activity carve-out.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the statute's 'engaged in criminal activity' limitation applies only to activity unrelated to the immediate confrontation" — wrong because the Florida statute provides no such "causal nexus" or "severance" exception; the bar is categorical for those engaged in criminal activity.
- (b): "regardless of safety" — wrong because NY Penal Law § 35.15(2)(a) explicitly states the duty applies only if the actor knows they can retreat with "complete personal safety."
- (c): "California applies the traditional retreat rule to all non-dwelling confrontations" — wrong because California common law (and CALCRIM 505) explicitly states a defendant has no duty to retreat in any place they have a right to be.
- (e): "The castle doctrine applies to the warehouse in all three jurisdictions" — wrong because New York does not recognize the workplace/commercial extension of the castle doctrine, and none of the three jurisdictions apply it to criminal participants.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q15.** Based on the standoff at the warehouse, assume a jury finds Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity" despite any potential defenses. Evaluate how the duty-to-retreat analysis would proceed across three jurisdictions: New York, Florida, and California.

(a) Kant is entitled to Stand Your Ground immunity in Florida regardless of the ongoing proceeds transfer, because the statute's "engaged in criminal activity" limitation applies only to activity unrelated to the immediate confrontation; Bentham's aggression severs any connection to the predicate robbery.

(b) The New York duty to retreat is not satisfied unless a reasonably prudent person would have retreated using any available escape route, regardless of safety; the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes an available route as a matter of law.

(c) In California, Kant has a duty to retreat because California applies the traditional retreat rule to all non-dwelling confrontations, and the warehouse is a commercial space.

(d) In New York, the duty to retreat applies only if retreat is available "with complete personal safety," and a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver likely does not qualify as a safe retreat; in Florida, Kant loses the statutory right to stand his ground because the statute excludes those "engaged in criminal activity," meaning he reverts to a duty to retreat; in California, there is no duty to retreat. <!-- correct -->

(e) The castle doctrine applies to the warehouse in all three jurisdictions because Kant was a business invitee, and all three recognize a workplace extension of the castle doctrine.

**Answer:** (d)

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (NY "complete safety" retreat rule; Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2) SYG "criminal activity" bar; CA no-duty-to-retreat rule; castle-doctrine scope).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that Florida courts impose a nexus requirement between the criminal activity and the confrontation to prevent absurd outcomes. Under this theory, if the criminal activity (receiving stolen goods) is completely passive and the attacker introduces the deadly threat independently, the causal chain is severed. Thus, Kant would retain his Stand Your Ground immunity "regardless of the ongoing proceeds transfer."
(b) Argument-for: A student might believe that "duty to retreat" jurisdictions impose an absolute requirement to attempt escape if a physical path exists. Under this view, the mere existence of an exit fifteen feet away creates an available route as a matter of law. Therefore, NY law would strictly demand retreat "regardless of safety," prioritizing flight over the use of deadly force.
(c) Argument-for: A student might assume that California, generally known for restrictive self-defense and firearms laws, follows the traditional common-law duty to retreat. Because the warehouse is a commercial space and Kant is not in his own home, the Castle Doctrine would not apply. Therefore, California would impose a strict duty to retreat in "all non-dwelling confrontations."
(d) Argument-for: This correctly synthesizes the self-defense laws of New York, Florida, and California. New York explicitly restricts the duty to retreat to situations where it can be done with "complete personal safety," which is objectively impossible when facing a drawn gun at six feet. Florida's Stand Your Ground statute (Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2)) expressly excludes individuals "engaged in criminal activity." California courts have established through case law (e.g., CALCRIM 505) that there is no duty to retreat anywhere one has a right to be.
(e) Argument-for: A student might argue that the Castle Doctrine has been broadly extended to workplaces and commercial spaces across most jurisdictions. Since Kant is a business invitee in the warehouse, he shares in the property's protected status. Under this logic, the Castle Doctrine categorically overrides the retreat requirement in "all three" jurisdictions because of a universal workplace extension.

Head-to-head: Option (d) is the only legally accurate choice, correctly stating NY's "complete personal safety" standard, FL's "criminal activity" statutory exception to Stand Your Ground, and CA's no-duty-to-retreat rule. Distractor (a) fabricates a legal "severance" doctrine for FL's SYG law, which is in reality a categorical bar when the defendant is engaged in criminal activity. Distractor (b) directly contradicts NY Penal Law § 35.15, which explicitly conditions the duty to retreat on the actor's ability to do so safely. Distractor (c) misstates California law entirely, falsely claiming CA follows the traditional retreat rule. Distractor (e) falsely claims a universal workplace Castle Doctrine extension applies to all three jurisdictions, ignoring that such extensions are not universal and do not protect invitees actively engaged in crimes. All distractors are cleanly falsifiable due to their use of absolute language.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the statute's 'engaged in criminal activity' limitation applies only to activity unrelated... Bentham's aggression severs any connection" — wrong because Florida's statutory bar on standing one's ground while engaged in criminal activity is a categorical exception with no such severance or nexus requirement.
- (b): "regardless of safety" — wrong because New York expressly requires retreat only if the defender knows they can do so with "complete personal safety."
- (c): "California applies the traditional retreat rule to all non-dwelling confrontations" — wrong because California is a no-duty-to-retreat state by case law and imposes no such duty in non-dwelling public confrontations.
- (e): "all three recognize a workplace extension of the castle doctrine" — wrong because there is no universal workplace extension applied in all three jurisdictions, particularly for an invitee actively committing a crime.

Recommended fix: none

---

## Q16

### Grounding

**Q16.** Assume that, whether or not Kant prevails on any other defense, he used defensive force against Officer Hand inside his Elm Street apartment after Officer Hand's lawful forced entry under a valid arrest warrant. Evaluate whether the castle doctrine authorizes Kant's defensive force against Officer Hand.

(a) The castle doctrine eliminates any duty to retreat from one's home but does not transform otherwise unjustified force into lawful self-defense; because Officer Hand was a lawful entrant executing a valid warrant after loudspeaker identification, his entry was not "unlawful force," and the predicate for any self-defense claim fails regardless of the castle doctrine's modification of the retreat duty. <!-- correct -->

(b) The castle doctrine authorizes defensive force against any person entering the home, including law-enforcement officers executing a warrant, because Cardozo's formulation in *People v. Tomlins* protects the dwelling without exception as to the identity of the intruder.

(c) The castle doctrine permits defensive force here because Florida's statutory presumption of reasonable fear applies to any in-home force claim and dispenses with the need to show that the entrant posed an actual imminent unlawful threat.

(d) The castle doctrine is unavailable because Kant was the initial aggressor in the warehouse encounter with Bentham, and the initial-aggressor bar follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations.

(e) The castle doctrine applies in every U.S. jurisdiction to permit defensive force against a person executing a warrant, because no state recognizes a "lawful entrant" exception to the sanctuary protection of the home.

**Answer:** (a)
**Explanation:** (a) correctly identifies the castle doctrine's structural limit. The doctrine eliminates a duty to retreat from one's home, but self-defense still requires that the aggressor be using "unlawful" force. Officer Hand's entry under a valid warrant, preceded by loudspeaker identification, is lawful by definition (Facts 11, 14). Without unlawful force, there is no self-defense predicate for the castle doctrine to modify. Whether Kant has any other defense (insanity, imperfect self-defense) is taken up separately in Q7–Q9. (b) misreads *Tomlins*, which addressed the duty to retreat, not the self-defense predicate of unlawful force. (c) falsely describes FL's statutory presumption; the presumption applies to unlawful or forcible entry, and entry under a valid warrant is lawful. (d) falsely carries initial-aggressor status across unrelated encounters; the initial-aggressor bar applies within a discrete confrontation, not as a life-long disability. (e) falsely universalizes castle-doctrine protection against lawful entrants; every jurisdiction treats lawful police entry as non-predicate for self-defense.
**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [castle-doctrine, lawful-entrant, initial-aggressor, unlawful-force-predicate], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis
**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I (*People v. Tomlins*, 213 N.Y. 240 (1914): castle doctrine eliminates retreat duty but requires underlying unlawful force).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — castle doctrine, Ch 22, Refinement `castle-doctrine-scope`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

### Edge Case Audit

**Q16.** Assume that, whether or not Kant prevails on any other defense, he used defensive force against Officer Hand inside his Elm Street apartment after Officer Hand's lawful forced entry under a valid arrest warrant. Evaluate whether the castle doctrine authorizes Kant's defensive force against Officer Hand.

(a) The castle doctrine eliminates any duty to retreat from one's home but does not transform otherwise unjustified force into lawful self-defense; because Officer Hand was a lawful entrant executing a valid warrant after loudspeaker identification, his entry was not "unlawful force," and the predicate for any self-defense claim fails regardless of the castle doctrine's modification of the retreat duty. <!-- correct -->

(b) The castle doctrine authorizes defensive force against any person entering the home, including law-enforcement officers executing a warrant, because Cardozo's formulation in *People v. Tomlins* protects the dwelling without exception as to the identity of the intruder.

(c) The castle doctrine permits defensive force here because Florida's statutory presumption of reasonable fear applies to any in-home force claim and dispenses with the need to show that the entrant posed an actual imminent unlawful threat.

(d) The castle doctrine is unavailable because Kant was the initial aggressor in the warehouse encounter with Bentham, and the initial-aggressor bar follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations.

(e) The castle doctrine applies in every U.S. jurisdiction to permit defensive force against a person executing a warrant, because no state recognizes a "lawful entrant" exception to the sanctuary protection of the home.

**Answer:** (a)
**Explanation:** (a) correctly identifies the castle doctrine's structural limit. The doctrine eliminates a duty to retreat from one's home, but self-defense still requires that the aggressor be using "unlawful" force. Officer Hand's entry under a valid warrant, preceded by loudspeaker identification, is lawful by definition (Facts 11, 14). Without unlawful force, there is no self-defense predicate for the castle doctrine to modify. Whether Kant has any other defense (insanity, imperfect self-defense) is taken up separately in Q7–Q9. (b) misreads *Tomlins*, which addressed the duty to retreat, not the self-defense predicate of unlawful force. (c) falsely describes FL's statutory presumption; the presumption applies to unlawful or forcible entry, and entry under a valid warrant is lawful. (d) falsely carries initial-aggressor status across unrelated encounters; the initial-aggressor bar applies within a discrete confrontation, not as a life-long disability. (e) falsely universalizes castle-doctrine protection against lawful entrants; every jurisdiction treats lawful police entry as non-predicate for self-defense.
**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [castle-doctrine, lawful-entrant, initial-aggressor, unlawful-force-predicate], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis
**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I (*People v. Tomlins*, 213 N.Y. 240 (1914): castle doctrine eliminates retreat duty but requires underlying unlawful force).
