### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q16.** Assume that, whether or not Kant prevails on any other defense, he used defensive force against Officer Hand inside his Elm Street apartment after Officer Hand's lawful forced entry under a valid arrest warrant. Evaluate whether the castle doctrine authorizes Kant's defensive force against Officer Hand.

(a) The castle doctrine eliminates any duty to retreat from one's home but does not transform otherwise unjustified force into lawful self-defense; because Officer Hand was a lawful entrant executing a valid warrant after loudspeaker identification, his entry was not "unlawful force," and the predicate for any self-defense claim fails regardless of the castle doctrine's modification of the retreat duty. <!-- correct -->

(b) The castle doctrine authorizes defensive force against any person entering the home, including law-enforcement officers executing a warrant, because Cardozo's formulation in *People v. Tomlins* protects the dwelling without exception as to the identity of the intruder.

(c) The castle doctrine permits defensive force here because Florida's statutory presumption of reasonable fear applies to any in-home force claim and dispenses with the need to show that the entrant posed an actual imminent unlawful threat.

(d) The castle doctrine is unavailable because Kant was the initial aggressor in the warehouse encounter with Bentham, and the initial-aggressor bar follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations.

(e) The castle doctrine applies in every U.S. jurisdiction to permit defensive force against a person executing a warrant, because no state recognizes a "lawful entrant" exception to the sanctuary protection of the home.

**Answer:** (a)
**Explanation:** (a) correctly identifies the castle doctrine's structural limit. The doctrine eliminates a duty to retreat from one's home, but self-defense still requires that the aggressor be using "unlawful" force. Officer Hand's entry under a valid warrant, preceded by loudspeaker identification, is lawful by definition (Facts 11, 14). Without unlawful force, there is no self-defense predicate for the castle doctrine to modify. Whether Kant has any other defense (insanity, imperfect self-defense) is taken up separately in Q7–Q9. (b) misreads *Tomlins*, which addressed the duty to retreat, not the self-defense predicate of unlawful force. (c) falsely describes FL's statutory presumption; the presumption applies to unlawful or forcible entry, and entry under a valid warrant is lawful. (d) falsely carries initial-aggressor status across unrelated encounters; the initial-aggressor bar applies within a discrete confrontation, not as a life-long disability. (e) falsely universalizes castle-doctrine protection against lawful entrants; every jurisdiction treats lawful police entry as non-predicate for self-defense.
**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [castle-doctrine, lawful-entrant, initial-aggressor, unlawful-force-predicate], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis
**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I (*People v. Tomlins*, 213 N.Y. 240 (1914): castle doctrine eliminates retreat duty but requires underlying unlawful force).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: The castle doctrine is an exception to the duty to retreat, not an expansion of the definition of justifiable force. Under *People v. Tomlins*, while one is not required to flee their own home, the use of force is still predicated on the presence of an unlawful threat. Because Officer Hand entered lawfully pursuant to a valid warrant and identified himself, there is no "unlawful force" to defend against, and the doctrine provides no shield for Kant's actions.

(b) Argument-for: *People v. Tomlins* famously declares that "a man's house is his castle," describing the home as a sanctuary. A student could argue that the logic of this doctrine is to provide absolute protection within the home, making the occupant's right to defensive force superior to the state's power of entry. This interpretation views the dwelling as a jurisdictional limit on all external force, suggesting that the sanctuary protection is categorical and does not fluctuate based on the official identity or legal status of the entrant.

(c) Argument-for: In jurisdictions like Florida, the castle doctrine is codified to include a statutory presumption of reasonable fear of death or great bodily harm for anyone who uses force against a person who has entered their dwelling. A student might argue that this presumption dispenses with the need to prove the actual nature of the threat because the law assumes the threat from the fact of entry alone. This reading focuses on the procedural advantage of the presumption as a substitute for the traditional "unlawful threat" analysis.

(d) Argument-for: The initial-aggressor doctrine bars an individual who provoked a confrontation from later claiming self-defense in that same chain of events. If the attempt to arrest Kant is viewed as a continuation of the warehouse encounter, Kant's initial aggression there would follow him into the apartment. This argument posits that a defendant cannot cleanse their status as an aggressor by simply moving to a new location while being actively pursued for the initial crime, making the castle doctrine irrelevant.

(e) Argument-for: One could argue that the sanctuary of the home is a universal principle of American law that supersedes specific statutory exceptions regarding police entry. This argument suggests that the "castle" status is absolute and that the legal system lacks any "lawful entrant" exception because such an exception would effectively abolish the doctrine's core purpose of protecting the home from state overreach. It positions the home as a space where even the execution of a warrant does not strip the occupant of the right to resist.

Head-to-head: (a) is the strongest and correct option because it identifies the necessary intersection of the retreat rule (castle doctrine) and the predicate requirement of "unlawful force." The castle doctrine only modifies the *where* (the home), not the *what* (unlawful force vs. lawful entry). (b) and (e) are legally false because they claim the castle doctrine is absolute; in reality, it never protects against lawful entrants like police with warrants. (c) is legally false because the Florida presumption specifically requires the entry to be "unlawful and forcible," and a warrant makes entry lawful. (d) is legally false because the initial-aggressor bar is restricted to the discrete confrontation in which it arose and does not follow a defendant indefinitely into subsequent encounters.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "protects the dwelling without exception as to the identity of the intruder" — wrong because the castle doctrine only applies when the intruder's force is "unlawful," and it does not apply to lawful entrants like police.
- (c): "dispenses with the need to show that the entrant posed an actual imminent unlawful threat" — wrong because even statutory presumptions (like Florida’s) require an "unlawful and forcible" entry to trigger the presumption in the first place.
- (d): "initial-aggressor bar follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations" — wrong because initial-aggressor status is encounter-specific and does not permanently disable the right to self-defense in future, unrelated confrontations.
- (e): "no state recognizes a 'lawful entrant' exception" — wrong because every jurisdiction recognizes that the castle doctrine does not permit force against officers executing a valid warrant.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q16.** Assume that, whether or not Kant prevails on any other defense, he used defensive force against Officer Hand inside his Elm Street apartment after Officer Hand's lawful forced entry under a valid arrest warrant. Evaluate whether the castle doctrine authorizes Kant's defensive force against Officer Hand.

(a) The castle doctrine eliminates any duty to retreat from one's home but does not transform otherwise unjustified force into lawful self-defense; because Officer Hand was a lawful entrant executing a valid warrant after loudspeaker identification, his entry was not "unlawful force," and the predicate for any self-defense claim fails regardless of the castle doctrine's modification of the retreat duty. <!-- correct -->

(b) The castle doctrine authorizes defensive force against any person entering the home, including law-enforcement officers executing a warrant, because Cardozo's formulation in *People v. Tomlins* protects the dwelling without exception as to the identity of the intruder.

(c) The castle doctrine permits defensive force here because Florida's statutory presumption of reasonable fear applies to any in-home force claim and dispenses with the need to show that the entrant posed an actual imminent unlawful threat.

(d) The castle doctrine is unavailable because Kant was the initial aggressor in the warehouse encounter with Bentham, and the initial-aggressor bar follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations.

(e) The castle doctrine applies in every U.S. jurisdiction to permit defensive force against a person executing a warrant, because no state recognizes a "lawful entrant" exception to the sanctuary protection of the home.

**Answer:** (a)
**Explanation:** (a) correctly identifies the castle doctrine's structural limit. The doctrine eliminates a duty to retreat from one's home, but self-defense still requires that the aggressor be using "unlawful" force. Officer Hand's entry under a valid warrant, preceded by loudspeaker identification, is lawful by definition (Facts 11, 14). Without unlawful force, there is no self-defense predicate for the castle doctrine to modify. Whether Kant has any other defense (insanity, imperfect self-defense) is taken up separately in Q7–Q9. (b) misreads *Tomlins*, which addressed the duty to retreat, not the self-defense predicate of unlawful force. (c) falsely describes FL's statutory presumption; the presumption applies to unlawful or forcible entry, and entry under a valid warrant is lawful. (d) falsely carries initial-aggressor status across unrelated encounters; the initial-aggressor bar applies within a discrete confrontation, not as a life-long disability. (e) falsely universalizes castle-doctrine protection against lawful entrants; every jurisdiction treats lawful police entry as non-predicate for self-defense.
**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [castle-doctrine, lawful-entrant, initial-aggressor, unlawful-force-predicate], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis
**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I (*People v. Tomlins*, 213 N.Y. 240 (1914): castle doctrine eliminates retreat duty but requires underlying unlawful force).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: The castle doctrine functions strictly as a geographic exception to the duty to retreat, not as an independent grant of authority to use violence. To validly claim self-defense, a defendant must always establish that they were facing an imminent threat of unlawful force. Since Officer Hand was executing a valid arrest warrant after proper loudspeaker identification, his entry was legally authorized and thus constituted lawful force. Consequently, the foundational predicate for self-defense is absent, rendering the castle doctrine's retreat exception entirely irrelevant to Kant's actions. This option perfectly captures the structural limits of self-defense doctrine.

(b) Argument-for: In *People v. Tomlins*, Judge Cardozo emphasized the absolute sanctity of the home, stating that a man assailed in his dwelling "may stand his ground, and resist the attack." A student might argue that this foundational formulation of the castle doctrine centers entirely on the location of the attack rather than the legal status of the intruder. Because the home is an inviolable sanctuary, any forced entry—even by law enforcement—can be perceived by the occupant as a life-threatening intrusion justifying defensive force. Under this reading, the castle doctrine provides a blanket authorization for defending the dwelling without exception for the intruder's identity.

(c) Argument-for: Modern Stand Your Ground laws, typified by Florida's statutory framework, significantly expand the common-law castle doctrine by creating a legal presumption of reasonable fear of imminent death or great bodily harm. A student could argue that this presumption is designed to be universally applicable to any forceful entry into a dwelling, specifically to relieve the homeowner of the burden of proving the intruder's intent or lawful status in the heat of the moment. By dispensing with the need to affirmatively prove an actual imminent unlawful threat, this interpretation suggests the castle doctrine automatically protects Kant's defensive force upon any forced entry into his home.

(d) Argument-for: The initial-aggressor rule operates as a strict bar to claiming self-defense unless the aggressor clearly communicates a withdrawal. A student could argue that Kant's continuous flight from the initial warehouse altercation to his apartment constituted a single, unbroken chain of events. Because Kant never successfully withdrew or communicated a withdrawal to the pursuing authorities, his status as the initial aggressor remained active throughout the chase. Under this theory, the initial-aggressor taint follows him across all subsequent confrontations, universally precluding him from invoking the castle doctrine against Officer Hand.

(e) Argument-for: The fundamental principle that "a man's home is his castle" is deeply entrenched in American legal tradition. A student might argue that the castle doctrine is recognized in every U.S. jurisdiction as an absolute protection of the home that supersedes even law enforcement authority. Because forced entry inherently creates a chaotic and dangerous situation for the occupant, the argument would assert that no state recognizes a "lawful entrant" exception when the home's threshold is violently breached. Therefore, the doctrine universally applies to permit defensive force against anyone, including an officer executing a warrant.

Head-to-head: Option (a) is the only legally accurate statement, correctly identifying that the castle doctrine removes the duty to retreat but cannot cure the lack of the "unlawful force" predicate required for self-defense. Options (b), (c), (d), and (e) are effectively falsified by their absolute claims. Option (b) falsely claims *Tomlins* created a rule "without exception as to the identity of the intruder," ignoring the foundational unlawful force requirement. Option (c) falsely claims Florida's statutory presumption applies to "any in-home force claim," when Florida statutes expressly exempt entry by law enforcement officers executing their official duties. Option (d) falsely asserts the initial-aggressor bar "follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations," when it actually applies only within the discrete confrontation with the original adversary or their immediate defenders. Option (e) falsely claims "every U.S. jurisdiction" allows force against warrant execution and that "no state recognizes a 'lawful entrant' exception," which blatantly contradicts universal self-defense predicate requirements.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "protects the dwelling without exception as to the identity of the intruder" — wrong because self-defense fundamentally requires the threat to be unlawful; a lawful entrant executing a valid warrant does not qualify as an unlawful threat.
- (c): "applies to any in-home force claim" — wrong because statutory presumptions like Florida's explicitly exempt known law enforcement officers who are legally entering a dwelling to perform official duties.
- (d): "follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations" — wrong because the initial-aggressor bar does not attach to a person as a permanent, universal disability; it applies narrowly per discrete encounter.
- (e): "every U.S. jurisdiction... because no state recognizes a 'lawful entrant' exception" — wrong because all jurisdictions require the predicate of unlawful force to claim self-defense, meaning every state effectively recognizes a lawful entrant exception.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q17

### Grounding

**Q17.** Assume that, whether or not Kant is found insane, he stabbed Officer Hand while genuinely believing, due entirely to a psychotic delusion, that Officer Hand was a demon dispatched to execute him. Evaluate Kant's eligibility for imperfect self-defense in California under *People v. Schuller* and *People v. Elmore*, and in a jurisdiction (e.g., New York) that rejects imperfect self-defense.

(a) Imperfect self-defense is available in California because Kant's genuine belief, though purely delusional, suffices under *Schuller* to negate malice; it is unavailable in New York, which rejects the doctrine.

(b) Imperfect self-defense is unavailable in California because an honest defensive belief based entirely on a psychotic delusion sounds exclusively in insanity rather than self-defense; it is likewise unavailable in New York, which rejects the doctrine altogether. <!-- correct -->

(c) Imperfect self-defense is available in both California and New York, because both jurisdictions treat any genuinely held defensive belief—even a purely delusional one—as negating malice and reducing the charge to attempted voluntary manslaughter.

(d) Imperfect self-defense reduces the charge to criminally negligent homicide in California because it substitutes a subjective-reasonableness standard; in New York, it serves as a complete justification.

(e) The Supreme Court's decision in *Kahler v. Kansas* requires every state to recognize imperfect self-defense for delusional beliefs, preempting California's and New York's state-law limitations.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) is correct because, under California law (*People v. Elmore*, reaffirmed in *People v. Schuller*), a belief in the need for self-defense that is based *entirely* on a psychotic delusion cannot support imperfect self-defense; such purely delusional claims sound exclusively in the insanity defense. New York categorically rejects the imperfect self-defense doctrine. (a) misstates California law; *Schuller* did not adopt a pure "genuineness-only" rule for pure delusions, which remain barred under *Elmore*. (c) misstates both California and New York law, as neither permits imperfect self-defense for a pure delusion. (d) misidentifies the doctrine's effect in California (it reduces murder to voluntary manslaughter, not criminally negligent homicide) and misstates New York law, which rejects the doctrine rather than treating it as a complete justification. (e) misreads *Kahler v. Kansas*, which held that due process imposes no specific baseline for mental-state defenses, contrary to the claim that it mandates imperfect self-defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [22, 23], topics: [imperfect-self-defense, Schuller-genuineness, Elmore-delusion, jurisdictional-variation, Kahler], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part III (*People v. Elmore*, 59 Cal.4th 121 (2014) & *People v. Schuller*, 15 Cal.5th 237 (2023): purely delusional beliefs barred from imperfect self-defense; Part I table: NY rejects imperfect self-defense); Chapter 23 (*Kahler v. Kansas*, 589 U.S. 271 (2020): due-process deference to state choice).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Imperfect self-defense (delusional beliefs), Ch 22, Refinement `imperfect-self-defense` and `schuller-genuineness-only`; Kahler due-process floor, Ch 23, Refinement `kahler-constitutional-floor`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q17.** Assume that, whether or not Kant is found insane, he stabbed Officer Hand while genuinely believing, due entirely to a psychotic delusion, that Officer Hand was a demon dispatched to execute him. Evaluate Kant's eligibility for imperfect self-defense in California under *People v. Schuller* and *People v. Elmore*, and in a jurisdiction (e.g., New York) that rejects imperfect self-defense.

(a) Imperfect self-defense is available in California because Kant's genuine belief, though purely delusional, suffices under *Schuller* to negate malice; it is unavailable in New York, which rejects the doctrine.

(b) Imperfect self-defense is unavailable in California because an honest defensive belief based entirely on a psychotic delusion sounds exclusively in insanity rather than self-defense; it is likewise unavailable in New York, which rejects the doctrine altogether. <!-- correct -->

(c) Imperfect self-defense is available in both California and New York, because both jurisdictions treat any genuinely held defensive belief—even a purely delusional one—as negating malice and reducing the charge to attempted voluntary manslaughter.

(d) Imperfect self-defense reduces the charge to criminally negligent homicide in California because it substitutes a subjective-reasonableness standard; in New York, it serves as a complete justification.

(e) The Supreme Court's decision in *Kahler v. Kansas* requires every state to recognize imperfect self-defense for delusional beliefs, preempting California's and New York's state-law limitations.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) is correct because, under California law (*People v. Elmore*, reaffirmed in *People v. Schuller*), a belief in the need for self-defense that is based *entirely* on a psychotic delusion cannot support imperfect self-defense; such purely delusional claims sound exclusively in the insanity defense. New York categorically rejects the imperfect self-defense doctrine. (a) misstates California law; *Schuller* did not adopt a pure "genuineness-only" rule for pure delusions, which remain barred under *Elmore*. (c) misstates both California and New York law, as neither permits imperfect self-defense for a pure delusion. (d) misidentifies the doctrine's effect in California (it reduces murder to voluntary manslaughter, not criminally negligent homicide) and misstates New York law, which rejects the doctrine rather than treating it as a complete justification. (e) misreads *Kahler v. Kansas*, which held that due process imposes no specific baseline for mental-state defenses, contrary to the claim that it mandates imperfect self-defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [22, 23], topics: [imperfect-self-defense, Schuller-genuineness, Elmore-delusion, jurisdictional-variation, Kahler], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part III (*People v. Elmore*, 59 Cal.4th 121 (2014) & *People v. Schuller*, 15 Cal.5th 237 (2023): purely delusional beliefs barred from imperfect self-defense; Part I table: NY rejects imperfect self-defense); Chapter 23 (*Kahler v. Kansas*, 589 U.S. 271 (2020): due-process deference to state choice).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under People v. Schuller (2023), the California Supreme Court clarified that imperfect self-defense is not a true affirmative defense but a doctrine that negates the "malice" element of murder. Because malice requires the absence of a belief in the need for self-defense, a defendant like Kant, who genuinely (even if delusionally) believes he is under threat, lacks the subjective state of mind necessary for murder. One could argue that because Schuller elevated the failure to instruct on this doctrine to a constitutional error, the "genuineness" of the belief is the only constitutionally relevant factor, potentially superseding the earlier limitation in Elmore regarding delusions. Thus, California would allow the defense while New York, which strictly adheres to a reasonable-belief standard for justification, would not.

(b) Argument-for: This option correctly applies the rule from People v. Elmore, which remains good law in California even after Schuller. Elmore held that while imperfect self-defense negates malice, it cannot be predicated on a "purely delusional" belief because such a belief has no basis in objective reality and is therefore the exclusive province of the insanity defense (M'Naghten) rather than the self-defense doctrine. Since Kant’s belief was based "entirely" on a psychotic delusion, he is ineligible for the defense in California. In New York, the doctrine is rejected categorically, as New York does not recognize an "unreasonable belief" as a mitigator for murder outside of the specific "Extreme Emotional Disturbance" (EED) defense.

(c) Argument-for: An advocate might argue that the fundamental principle of mens rea requires that a person cannot be convicted of murder if they lack the subjective intent to act unlawfully. If Kant genuinely believed he was defending his life against a demon, he lacked the "malignant heart" or intent to kill a human being unlawfully. In this view, both California and New York must provide a mechanism—whether called imperfect self-defense or interpreted through the lens of voluntary manslaughter—to ensure that a defendant’s actual subjective state of mind is accounted for, preventing a murder conviction where the defendant believed their actions were justified.

(d) Argument-for: This argument posits that imperfect self-defense functions as a bridge between intentional murder and accidental killing. In California, by proving a subjective (but unreasonable) belief in the need for force, the defendant demonstrates a level of culpability closer to criminal negligence than to malice. In New York, one could argue that the state's justification statutes, when read broadly in light of due process, should function as a complete justification if the defendant can prove they acted without any criminal intent, effectively treating a "genuine" belief as a total shield to liability.

(e) Argument-for: In Kahler v. Kansas (2020), the Supreme Court held that states have the power to define the insanity defense, but it also suggested that states must allow defendants to introduce evidence of mental illness to negate the mens rea of a crime. A student could argue that Kahler implies a constitutional mandate: if a delusion negates the specific intent or malice required for murder, the state cannot categorically bar that evidence. Under this reading, Kahler would preempt state-law restrictions like California’s Elmore rule or New York’s rejection of the doctrine, forcing every state to recognize a "delusional belief" as a mitigator or defense.

Head-to-head: (b) is the strongest answer because it identifies the precise legal boundary established in People v. Elmore—that purely delusional beliefs are relegated to the insanity phase and cannot support a claim of imperfect self-defense during the guilt phase. (a) is weaker because it interprets People v. Schuller as expanding the doctrine to delusions, when Schuller actually focused on the procedural standard of review for instructional error. (c) is factually incorrect regarding New York law, which has explicitly rejected the imperfect self-defense doctrine for decades. (d) misstates the legal effect of the doctrine in California (which reduces murder to manslaughter, not negligent homicide) and mischaracterizes New York's justification law. (e) is a significant misreading of Kahler v. Kansas, which actually affirmed state sovereignty in defining the insanity defense rather than imposing a federal mandate for specific mitigations like imperfect self-defense.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "Imperfect self-defense is available in California because Kant's genuine belief... suffices under Schuller to negate malice" — wrong because Elmore (reaffirmed by Schuller) holds that purely delusional beliefs cannot support imperfect self-defense.
- (c): "available in both California and New York" — wrong because New York does not recognize the doctrine and California excludes purely delusional beliefs.
- (d): "reduces the charge to criminally negligent homicide in California" — wrong because the doctrine reduces murder to voluntary manslaughter.
- (e): "Kahler v. Kansas requires every state to recognize imperfect self-defense" — wrong because Kahler held that the Constitution does not require any specific version of the insanity defense, let alone a specific mitigation doctrine for delusions.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q17.** Assume that, whether or not Kant is found insane, he stabbed Officer Hand while genuinely believing, due entirely to a psychotic delusion, that Officer Hand was a demon dispatched to execute him. Evaluate Kant's eligibility for imperfect self-defense in California under *People v. Schuller* and *People v. Elmore*, and in a jurisdiction (e.g., New York) that rejects imperfect self-defense.

(a) Imperfect self-defense is available in California because Kant's genuine belief, though purely delusional, suffices under *Schuller* to negate malice; it is unavailable in New York, which rejects the doctrine.

(b) Imperfect self-defense is unavailable in California because an honest defensive belief based entirely on a psychotic delusion sounds exclusively in insanity rather than self-defense; it is likewise unavailable in New York, which rejects the doctrine altogether. <!-- correct -->

(c) Imperfect self-defense is available in both California and New York, because both jurisdictions treat any genuinely held defensive belief—even a purely delusional one—as negating malice and reducing the charge to attempted voluntary manslaughter.

(d) Imperfect self-defense reduces the charge to criminally negligent homicide in California because it substitutes a subjective-reasonableness standard; in New York, it serves as a complete justification.

(e) The Supreme Court's decision in *Kahler v. Kansas* requires every state to recognize imperfect self-defense for delusional beliefs, preempting California's and New York's state-law limitations.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) is correct because, under California law (*People v. Elmore*, reaffirmed in *People v. Schuller*), a belief in the need for self-defense that is based *entirely* on a psychotic delusion cannot support imperfect self-defense; such purely delusional claims sound exclusively in the insanity defense. New York categorically rejects the imperfect self-defense doctrine. (a) misstates California law; *Schuller* did not adopt a pure "genuineness-only" rule for pure delusions, which remain barred under *Elmore*. (c) misstates both California and New York law, as neither permits imperfect self-defense for a pure delusion. (d) misidentifies the doctrine's effect in California (it reduces murder to voluntary manslaughter, not criminally negligent homicide) and misstates New York law, which rejects the doctrine rather than treating it as a complete justification. (e) misreads *Kahler v. Kansas*, which held that due process imposes no specific baseline for mental-state defenses, contrary to the claim that it mandates imperfect self-defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [22, 23], topics: [imperfect-self-defense, Schuller-genuineness, Elmore-delusion, jurisdictional-variation, Kahler], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part III (*People v. Elmore*, 59 Cal.4th 121 (2014) & *People v. Schuller*, 15 Cal.5th 237 (2023): purely delusional beliefs barred from imperfect self-defense; Part I table: NY rejects imperfect self-defense); Chapter 23 (*Kahler v. Kansas*, 589 U.S. 271 (2020): due-process deference to state choice).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that because imperfect self-defense is fundamentally about the subjective negation of malice, *Schuller*'s emphasis on a defendant's actual belief logically encompasses delusions. If the belief is genuine, regardless of its psychotic origin, it negates the malice aforethought required for murder, making imperfect self-defense available in California while correctly noting New York's rejection of the doctrine.
(b) Argument-for: This is the structurally correct application of California and New York law. Under *People v. Elmore*, a defensive belief arising entirely from a psychotic delusion sounds exclusively in the insanity defense, not imperfect self-defense, a rule that remains intact under *Schuller*. Furthermore, New York categorically rejects the imperfect self-defense doctrine, making the defense unavailable in both jurisdictions for this scenario.
(c) Argument-for: A student might reason that modern mens rea principles strictly require malice for murder, meaning any genuine belief in the need for self-defense—even if entirely delusional—must negate malice. This would lead to the conclusion that both California and New York treat such a subjective mental state as incompatible with murder, universally reducing the charge to manslaughter or attempted manslaughter.
(d) Argument-for: A student could believe that because an imperfect self-defense claim is inherently based on an objectively unreasonable (negligent) belief, California law explicitly maps the offense to criminally negligent homicide rather than voluntary manslaughter. Additionally, they might mistakenly conflate New York law with a completely subjective justification standard under the Model Penal Code.
(e) Argument-for: A student could interpret the Supreme Court's ruling in *Kahler v. Kansas* as establishing a constitutional floor for presenting evidence of mental illness to negate mens rea elements. Under this view, *Kahler* categorically requires that states allow defendants to use purely delusional beliefs to negate malice, thereby preempting California and New York's state-law limitations.

Head-to-head: (b) accurately synthesizes California's limitation on imperfect self-defense (barring pure delusions under *Elmore*) and New York's complete rejection of the doctrine. The distractors all rely on clear misstatements of black-letter law or Supreme Court precedent. (a) misstates California law by claiming pure delusions categorically negate malice under *Schuller*. (c) falsely claims both states recognize the doctrine for pure delusions. (d) misidentifies the resulting mitigated charge in California and incorrectly invents New York's stance. (e) completely reverses the holding of *Kahler*, which afforded states broad due-process deference. The distractors all contain locked, falsifiable errors. The question is mathematically sound and conceptually rigorous.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "suffices under Schuller to negate malice" — wrong because California law (*Elmore*) explicitly bars imperfect self-defense based entirely on a psychotic delusion.
- (c): "both jurisdictions treat any genuinely held defensive belief... as negating malice" — wrong because New York categorically rejects imperfect self-defense and California bars it for pure delusions.
- (d): "reduces the charge to criminally negligent homicide in California ... in New York, it serves as a complete justification" — wrong because it mitigates murder to voluntary manslaughter in California, and New York categorically rejects the doctrine rather than treating it as a complete justification.
- (e): "requires every state to recognize imperfect self-defense for delusional beliefs" — wrong because *Kahler* held that the Due Process Clause does not compel states to adopt specific insanity or mental-illness baseline defenses.

Recommended fix: none

## Q18

### Grounding

**Q18.** Assume that at the time of the stabbing, Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia) in an active psychotic episode. As established by Dr. Singer's testimony, Kant knew the penal code prohibited killing a human being, but his delusion convinced him he was morally required to destroy a demon possessing Officer Hand's body. Apply the M'Naghten wrongness prong to Kant's conduct, distinguishing jurisdictions that define wrongness morally from those that define it legally.

(a) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited, and Kant's violent resistance to a uniformed officer demonstrates knowledge that the stabbing was illegal.
(b) Kant satisfies M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong because his delusion distorted his perception of Officer Hand; under M'Naghten, the nature-and-quality prong categorically evaluates the defendant's moral understanding of the victim's identity rather than the physical mechanics of the act.
(c) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because 18 U.S.C. § 17 requires a "severe" mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public; paranoid schizophrenia alone, without that dangerousness showing, is insufficient to meet the federal standard.
(d) Kant is constitutionally entitled to an acquittal because *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Due Process Clause requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong as a floor for insanity defenses.
(e) Kant satisfies the wrongness prong under M'Naghten's moral-wrongfulness formulation because his delusion rendered him unable to recognize the stabbing as morally wrong, believing it was morally required to defeat the demonic attack; in jurisdictions that limit wrongness to legal wrongfulness, Kant fails the prong because he understood the penal code prohibited killing a human being. <!-- correct -->

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) correctly applies M'Naghten's wrongness prong based on the recognized jurisdictional split. Under a moral formulation, Kant's delusion provided a subjective moral justification, satisfying the prong. Under a legal formulation, he fails the prong because he knew killing the possessed human host was legally prohibited. (a) falsely claims the wrongness prong uniformly demands legal wrongfulness, ignoring the split. (b) states a categorically false legal rule; the "nature and quality" prong evaluates the physical nature of the act (e.g., physically stabbing a living human versus squeezing a grapefruit), not the moral understanding of the victim's identity. (c) fabricates a "specific danger to the public" element for the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act. (d) inverts *Kahler v. Kansas*, which explicitly held that due process does not require states to adopt the M'Naghten wrongness test.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [M'Naghten, wrongness-prong, moral-vs-legal-wrongfulness, nature-and-quality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Part A (M'Naghten's two prongs); Part D (post-Hinckley reforms); Kahler v. Kansas, 589 U.S. 271 (2020) (cataloguing cognitive/moral strains).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — M'Naghten wrongness, Ch 23, Refinement `mnaghten-wrongness`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q18.** Assume that at the time of the stabbing, Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia) in an active psychotic episode. As established by Dr. Singer's testimony, Kant knew the penal code prohibited killing a human being, but his delusion convinced him he was morally required to destroy a demon possessing Officer Hand's body. Apply the M'Naghten wrongness prong to Kant's conduct, distinguishing jurisdictions that define wrongness morally from those that define it legally.

(a) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited, and Kant's violent resistance to a uniformed officer demonstrates knowledge that the stabbing was illegal.
(b) Kant satisfies M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong because his delusion distorted his perception of Officer Hand; under M'Naghten, the nature-and-quality prong categorically evaluates the defendant's moral understanding of the victim's identity rather than the physical mechanics of the act.
(c) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because 18 U.S.C. § 17 requires a "severe" mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public; paranoid schizophrenia alone, without that dangerousness showing, is insufficient to meet the federal standard.
(d) Kant is constitutionally entitled to an acquittal because *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Due Process Clause requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong as a floor for insanity defenses.
(e) Kant satisfies the wrongness prong under M'Naghten's moral-wrongfulness formulation because his delusion rendered him unable to recognize the stabbing as morally wrong, believing it was morally required to defeat the demonic attack; in jurisdictions that limit wrongness to legal wrongfulness, Kant fails the prong because he understood the penal code prohibited killing a human being.

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) correctly applies M'Naghten's wrongness prong based on the recognized jurisdictional split. Under a moral formulation, Kant's delusion provided a subjective moral justification, satisfying the prong. Under a legal formulation, he fails the prong because he knew killing the possessed human host was legally prohibited. (a) falsely claims the wrongness prong uniformly demands legal wrongfulness, ignoring the split. (b) states a categorically false legal rule; the "nature and quality" prong evaluates the physical nature of the act (e.g., physically stabbing a living human versus squeezing a grapefruit), not the moral understanding of the victim's identity. (c) fabricates a "specific danger to the public" element for the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act. (d) inverts *Kahler v. Kansas*, which explicitly held that due process does not require states to adopt the M'Naghten wrongness test.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [M'Naghten, wrongness-prong, moral-vs-legal-wrongfulness, nature-and-quality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Part A (M'Naghten's two prongs); Part D (post-Hinckley reforms); Kahler v. Kansas, 589 U.S. 271 (2020) (cataloguing cognitive/moral strains).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under the legal interpretation of the M'Naghten test, "wrong" is strictly equated with "illegal." In jurisdictions following the reasoning in cases like *State v. Crenshaw*, a defendant’s subjective belief in a moral or religious duty does not excuse their conduct if they are aware that society, through its penal code, has prohibited the act. Because Kant acknowledged that the penal code prohibited killing a human being and his actions against a uniformed officer showed an awareness of the legal consequences, he fails the "wrongness" prong. This argument posits that the law serves as the uniform, objective standard for wrongness across these jurisdictions.

(b) Argument-for: The "nature and quality" prong of M'Naghten focuses on the defendant's cognitive understanding of what they are physically doing. If a defendant believes they are attacking a demon, they lack the cognitive realization that they are actually stabbing a human being. One could argue that the identity of the victim is an essential "quality" of the act, meaning Kant did not truly understand the nature of his conduct. By distorting the identity of the target from an officer to a demon, the delusion prevented Kant from comprehending the physical and moral reality of the stabbing.

(c) Argument-for: The federal Insanity Defense Reform Act, codified at 18 U.S.C. § 17, significantly narrowed the insanity defense. It requires the defendant to prove by "clear and convincing evidence" that a "severe" mental disease rendered them unable to appreciate the nature or wrongness of their acts. A student could argue that federal courts interpret the "severity" and "appreciation" requirements in light of the defendant's overall danger to the public. If Kant cannot demonstrate that his condition specifically bypasses the high evidentiary bar of clear-and-convincing proof regarding his lack of appreciation, his paranoid schizophrenia alone is legally insufficient to secure an acquittal under the federal standard.

(d) Argument-for: In *Kahler v. Kansas*, the Supreme Court engaged in a deep historical analysis of the insanity defense. An advocate might argue that the Court’s discussion of the "cognitive" and "moral" prongs of M'Naghten suggests that these elements are so deeply rooted in the American legal tradition as to be fundamental to due process. If the "wrongness" prong is a historical necessity for a fair trial, then any state that fails to offer it would be acting unconstitutionally. Under this interpretation, Kant's inability to perceive the moral wrongness of his act due to psychosis must be recognized by any state, regardless of its specific statutory language.

(e) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the bifurcation in American law regarding the definition of "wrongfulness" in the M'Naghten test. In jurisdictions that apply a "moral" standard (such as the *deific decree* or *subjective moral* exceptions), Kant’s belief that he was morally required to kill the demon satisfies the test because he could not appreciate the moral wrongness of the act. However, in jurisdictions that limit the inquiry to "legal" wrongness, Kant's knowledge that the act was a violation of the penal code remains the decisive factor. This option is the most accurate because it accounts for both sides of the jurisdictional split.

Head-to-head: (e) is the only option that accurately reflects the nuances of the M'Naghten "wrongness" prong and the modern jurisdictional split between moral and legal interpretations. Option (a) fails because it uses the word "uniformly," ignoring the significant number of states that use a moral standard. Option (b) mischaracterizes the "nature and quality" prong, which traditionally focuses on the physical mechanics of the act (e.g., believing a throat is a lemon) rather than the moral identity of the victim. Option (c) invents a "specific danger to the public" requirement that does not exist in 18 U.S.C. § 17. Option (d) is a direct legal error, as *Kahler v. Kansas* held that states are *not* constitutionally required to adopt the M'Naghten moral-wrongness test.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited" — wrong because many jurisdictions (e.g., NY, CA) define wrongness morally, not legally.
- (b): "nature-and-quality prong categorically evaluates the defendant's moral understanding of the victim's identity rather than the physical mechanics" — wrong because the prong focuses on the physical nature of the act (the "lemon" or "grapefruit" examples).
- (c): "18 U.S.C. § 17 requires... proof of a specific danger to the public" — wrong because § 17 contains no such public-danger element for the insanity defense itself.
- (d): "*Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Due Process Clause requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong" — wrong because *Kahler* held that Kansas could constitutionally eliminate the "moral wrongness" prong.

Recommended fix: none

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q18.** Assume that at the time of the stabbing, Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia) in an active psychotic episode. As established by Dr. Singer's testimony, Kant knew the penal code prohibited killing a human being, but his delusion convinced him he was morally required to destroy a demon possessing Officer Hand's body. Apply the M'Naghten wrongness prong to Kant's conduct, distinguishing jurisdictions that define wrongness morally from those that define it legally.

(a) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited, and Kant's violent resistance to a uniformed officer demonstrates knowledge that the stabbing was illegal.
(b) Kant satisfies M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong because his delusion distorted his perception of Officer Hand; under M'Naghten, the nature-and-quality prong categorically evaluates the defendant's moral understanding of the victim's identity rather than the physical mechanics of the act.
(c) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because 18 U.S.C. § 17 requires a "severe" mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public; paranoid schizophrenia alone, without that dangerousness showing, is insufficient to meet the federal standard.
(d) Kant is constitutionally entitled to an acquittal because *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Due Process Clause requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong as a floor for insanity defenses.
(e) Kant satisfies the wrongness prong under M'Naghten's moral-wrongfulness formulation because his delusion rendered him unable to recognize the stabbing as morally wrong, believing it was morally required to defeat the demonic attack; in jurisdictions that limit wrongness to legal wrongfulness, Kant fails the prong because he understood the penal code prohibited killing a human being. <!-- correct -->

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) correctly applies M'Naghten's wrongness prong based on the recognized jurisdictional split. Under a moral formulation, Kant's delusion provided a subjective moral justification, satisfying the prong. Under a legal formulation, he fails the prong because he knew killing the possessed human host was legally prohibited. (a) falsely claims the wrongness prong uniformly demands legal wrongfulness, ignoring the split. (b) states a categorically false legal rule; the "nature and quality" prong evaluates the physical nature of the act (e.g., physically stabbing a living human versus squeezing a grapefruit), not the moral understanding of the victim's identity. (c) fabricates a "specific danger to the public" element for the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act. (d) inverts *Kahler v. Kansas*, which explicitly held that due process does not require states to adopt the M'Naghten wrongness test.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [M'Naghten, wrongness-prong, moral-vs-legal-wrongfulness, nature-and-quality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Part A (M'Naghten's two prongs); Part D (post-Hinckley reforms); Kahler v. Kansas, 589 U.S. 271 (2020) (cataloguing cognitive/moral strains).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that the traditional M'Naghten rule, originating in 19th-century England, originally focused on the defendant's knowledge that the act was contrary to the law of the land. Under this strict reading, an individual's personal moral code or delusions cannot override their cognitive awareness of a criminal statute. Because Kant knew the penal code prohibited killing humans, his cognitive awareness of the act's illegality is unquestionable. Thus, a student could conclude the wrongness prong uniformly forecloses the defense whenever legal prohibition is known.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that "nature and quality" encompasses a defendant's comprehension of what they are actually destroying. If a defendant truly believes they are striking a supernatural entity (a demon) rather than a human being, they do not understand the true nature of their physical target. This interpretation stretches the "nature" of the act to include the ontological status of the victim. Therefore, a student could conclude that the nature-and-quality prong categorically evaluates this delusional misidentification over mere physical mechanics.
(c) Argument-for: A student might argue that the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17) drastically narrowed the insanity defense post-Hinckley, shifting the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. Given the federal system's emphasis on public protection and stringent commitment procedures, a student might incorrectly assume the statute explicitly incorporated a dangerousness element into the substantive definition of insanity itself. Therefore, the student would argue that schizophrenia without a statutory showing of specific danger to the public fails the federal standard.
(d) Argument-for: A student could argue that due process sets a fundamental constitutional floor for criminal culpability, of which mens rea and moral blameworthiness are essential components. Because Kahler v. Kansas dealt with a state's attempt to modify the insanity defense, a student might misremember the holding as striking down the Kansas law to protect the traditional M'Naghten wrongness prong. Under this mistaken reading, the Court established that states cannot constitutionally punish those who lack moral understanding of their actions.
(e) Argument-for: This option accurately reflects the recognized jurisdictional split regarding the interpretation of "wrong" under the M'Naghten test. In jurisdictions like New York (following People v. Schmidt), "wrong" means morally wrong, allowing for an insanity defense if a delusion makes the act seem morally justified (e.g., deific decree or demonic possession). In jurisdictions where "wrong" strictly means legally wrong (e.g., State v. Crenshaw), the defendant's knowledge of the penal code defeats the defense. Kant's precise mix of legal knowledge and moral delusion perfectly illustrates how his fate depends entirely on the jurisdiction's specific definition of wrongfulness.

Head-to-head: Option (e) is the clear winner because it accurately applies the well-established jurisdictional split over M'Naghten's "wrongness" prong to the fact pattern. Option (a) fails by using the absolute word "uniformly," denying the existence of the moral-wrongness jurisdictions. Option (b) fails by explicitly rejecting the core premise of the "nature and quality" prong (physical mechanics). Option (c) hallucinates a statutory element ("specific danger to the public") for 18 U.S.C. § 17. Option (d) asserts the precise opposite of the Supreme Court's actual holding in Kahler v. Kansas.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited" — wrong because it ignores a major jurisdictional split; many states define wrongness as moral wrongness, not legal wrongness.
- (b): "categorically evaluates the defendant's moral understanding of the victim's identity rather than the physical mechanics" — wrong because the "nature and quality" prong is precisely an evaluation of the physical mechanics and nature of the act, not moral understanding.
- (c): "requires a 'severe' mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public" — wrong because 18 U.S.C. § 17 does not require proof of a specific danger to the public as an element of the insanity defense.
- (d): "Kahler v. Kansas held that the Due Process Clause requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong" — wrong because Kahler held that Due Process does not compel states to adopt the M'Naghten test or its moral incapacity prong.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q19

### Grounding

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would prevail because the federal statute adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984, and Dr. Laplace's finding that Kant was "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" tracks that statutory language.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the requirements of law—and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant must show (1) a "severe" mental disease, (2) inability (not merely substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature and quality or wrongfulness of the act, and (3) proof by clear and convincing evidence; the volitional prong is unavailable. <!-- correct -->
(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct, and Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is insufficient because substantial impairment falls short of the total incapacity the Model Penal Code requires.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the Insanity Defense Reform Act, the burden rests on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence, and Dr. Laplace's testimony satisfies that standard uniformly across jurisdictions.
(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was held unconstitutional by *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that due process precludes any insanity defense that extends beyond negation of mens rea; the volitional prong is therefore no longer available in any jurisdiction.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The Model Penal Code requires only that the defendant lack substantial capacity to either appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs. The Insanity Defense Reform Act tightened the federal standard, requiring "severe" mental disease, total inability to appreciate wrongfulness, dropping the volitional prong entirely, and shifting the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states the IDRA adopted the volitional prong; it eliminated it. (c) falsely elevates the MPC requirement to total incapacity, whereas the MPC only requires "substantial capacity." (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [Model Penal Code-substantial-capacity, volitional-prong, federal-IDRA, burden-of-proof], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on *Kahler v. Kansas*.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Insanity Defense Standards (MPC vs IDRA), Ch 23, Refinement `mpc-appreciate-criminality`, `mpc-conform-conduct`, `federal-idra-requirements`, `burden-allocation-post-hinckley`, and `kahler-constitutional-floor`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would prevail because the federal statute adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984, and Dr. Laplace's finding that Kant was "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" tracks that statutory language.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the requirements of law—and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant must show (1) a "severe" mental disease, (2) inability (not merely substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature and quality or wrongfulness of the act, and (3) proof by clear and convincing evidence; the volitional prong is unavailable.
(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct, and Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is insufficient because substantial impairment falls short of the total incapacity the Model Penal Code requires.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the Insanity Defense Reform Act, the burden rests on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence, and Dr. Laplace's testimony satisfies that standard uniformly across jurisdictions.
(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was held unconstitutional by *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that due process precludes any insanity defense that extends beyond negation of mens rea; the volitional prong is therefore no longer available in any jurisdiction.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The Model Penal Code requires only that the defendant lack substantial capacity to either appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs. The Insanity Defense Reform Act tightened the federal standard, requiring "severe" mental disease, total inability to appreciate wrongfulness, dropping the volitional prong entirely, and shifting the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states the IDRA adopted the volitional prong; it eliminated it. (c) falsely elevates the MPC requirement to total incapacity, whereas the MPC only requires "substantial capacity." (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [Model Penal Code-substantial-capacity, volitional-prong, federal-IDRA, burden-of-proof], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on *Kahler v. Kansas*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that the Insanity Defense Reform Act of 1984 sought to codify a modern standard for insanity and that the phrasing "unable to conform his conduct" is so fundamentally linked to modern insanity jurisprudence that it must have been included. Given that Dr. Laplace used the exact language of the volitional prong, the student might conclude this evidence is the "gold standard" for a federal defense. Furthermore, the 1984 Act did significantly revise federal law, and one could mistake the direction of that revision as being more inclusive rather than more restrictive.

(b) Argument-for: This option accurately details the structural differences between the MPC and federal law. The MPC uses a "substantial capacity" standard and includes a volitional prong (control), while the IDRA (18 U.S.C. § 17) requires a "severe" disease, uses a cognitive-only test ("unable to appreciate"), and explicitly places the burden on the defendant by the heightened "clear and convincing" standard. Dr. Laplace's findings satisfy both MPC prongs (substantial impairment of appreciation and inability to conform) but might fail the IDRA's cognitive test if "substantial impairment" is interpreted as something less than a total "inability" to appreciate.

(c) Argument-for: This argument focuses on a strict literal reading of the MPC's language compared to Dr. Laplace's findings. One might argue that the MPC's "lack of substantial capacity" is a high bar that essentially functions as a requirement for total incapacity in practice. If Dr. Laplace only found "substantial impairment" of appreciation rather than a total lack of capacity, a court might find the evidence insufficient to overcome the presumption of sanity. This interprets "substantial impairment" as a weaker medical finding that does not rise to the level of the legal standard.

(d) Argument-for: In many criminal law contexts, the preponderance of the evidence standard is the default for affirmative defenses. A student might argue that both the MPC and federal law follow this general rule to avoid placing an unconstitutional or overly burdensome requirement on a mentally ill defendant. Because Dr. Laplace is a court-appointed expert, his testimony provides a neutral, expert basis that would likely satisfy a preponderance standard in almost any jurisdiction, making the distinction between tests practically irrelevant for a defendant with Kant's severe symptoms.

(e) Argument-for: *Kahler v. Kansas* (2020) is the most recent Supreme Court case regarding the insanity defense. A student might interpret the holding—that Kansas could constitutionally eliminate the "moral incapacity" (wrongfulness) prong—as a signal that the Due Process Clause only requires a defense based on the negation of mens rea. From this, one could argue that *Kahler* effectively invalidated the more expansive "volitional" prongs of the MPC by ruling they are not constitutionally required and that jurisdictions are now moving toward the minimal "mens rea" approach exclusively.

Head-to-head: (b) is the strongest because it correctly identifies the three-part tightening of the federal insanity defense (severity requirement, cognitive-only, and clear-and-convincing burden) following the Hinckley acquittal. (a) is factually inverted; the IDRA abolished the volitional prong. (c) misstates the MPC standard; the MPC famously lowered the bar from the M’Naghten "total" incapacity to "substantial" capacity. (d) is legally incorrect because the IDRA explicitly mandates the clear and convincing evidence standard (18 U.S.C. § 17(b)), which is higher than preponderance. (e) misreads *Kahler*; the Court held that the Constitution does not *require* a specific insanity test, but it certainly did not hold that states are *precluded* from using the MPC’s volitional prong.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the federal statute adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984" — wrong because the 1984 IDRA explicitly removed the volitional prong.
- (c): "substantial impairment falls short of the total incapacity the Model Penal Code requires" — wrong because the MPC only requires a lack of "substantial capacity," not "total incapacity."
- (d): "the burden rests on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence... uniformly across jurisdictions" — wrong because 18 U.S.C. § 17(b) requires clear and convincing evidence.
- (e): "Kahler v. Kansas... ruled that due process precludes any insanity defense that extends beyond negation of mens rea" — wrong because Kahler held that a mens-rea-only approach is constitutionally *permissible*, not that it is the *exclusive* constitutional limit.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would prevail because the federal statute adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984, and Dr. Laplace's finding that Kant was "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" tracks that statutory language.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the requirements of law—and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant must show (1) a "severe" mental disease, (2) inability (not merely substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature and quality or wrongfulness of the act, and (3) proof by clear and convincing evidence; the volitional prong is unavailable. <!-- correct -->
(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct, and Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is insufficient because substantial impairment falls short of the total incapacity the Model Penal Code requires.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the Insanity Defense Reform Act, the burden rests on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence, and Dr. Laplace's testimony satisfies that standard uniformly across jurisdictions.
(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was held unconstitutional by *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that due process precludes any insanity defense that extends beyond negation of mens rea; the volitional prong is therefore no longer available in any jurisdiction.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The Model Penal Code requires only that the defendant lack substantial capacity to either appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs. The Insanity Defense Reform Act tightened the federal standard, requiring "severe" mental disease, total inability to appreciate wrongfulness, dropping the volitional prong entirely, and shifting the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states the IDRA adopted the volitional prong; it eliminated it. (c) falsely elevates the MPC requirement to total incapacity, whereas the MPC only requires "substantial capacity." (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [Model Penal Code-substantial-capacity, volitional-prong, federal-IDRA, burden-of-proof], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on *Kahler v. Kansas*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might recall that the Insanity Defense Reform Act (IDRA) of 1984 comprehensively restructured the federal insanity test following the John Hinckley Jr. verdict. Conflating the MPC's widespread adoption in the mid-20th century with the federal statute, they could incorrectly conclude that the IDRA adopted the MPC's progressive "volitional prong," perfectly matching Dr. Laplace's diagnosis of Kant's inability to conform his conduct.
(b) Argument-for: This accurately recites both standards. The MPC allows a defense where the defendant lacks "substantial capacity" to either appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct. Kant's "unable to conform" and "substantially impaired" appreciation meet these definitions. Conversely, the IDRA of 1984 explicitly narrowed the defense by requiring a "severe" mental disease, eliminating the volitional prong, returning to a strict cognitive "inability" standard rather than "substantial impairment," and shifting the burden of proof to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that affirmative defenses like insanity are strictly construed to prevent abuse. Believing that all modern legal insanity tests are fundamentally rooted in the rigid cognitive demands of M'Naghten, they might falsely assume that the Model Penal Code also requires total "inability" and that a mere "substantial impairment" is legally insufficient to ground a complete excuse.
(d) Argument-for: The preponderance of the evidence is the standard burden of proof for most affirmative defenses in criminal law (like self-defense or duress in many states). A student might logically, though incorrectly, presume that the MPC and the IDRA uniformly standardized this evidentiary burden, meaning Kant would only need to prove his insanity by a preponderance of the evidence in either jurisdiction.
(e) Argument-for: In *Kahler v. Kansas*, the Supreme Court held that the Due Process Clause does not compel a state to adopt an insanity test that hinges on moral incapacity or the volitional prong. A student could mistakenly read this holding as an aggressive constitutional prohibition, arguing that *Kahler* actively invalidated the MPC's volitional prong on due process grounds.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the clear and legally precise correct answer, properly separating the dual-pronged "substantial capacity" test of the MPC from the strict, cognitive-only, clear-and-convincing test of the IDRA. The distractors all rely on demonstrable and falsifiable errors of black-letter law regarding these standard statutes and cases. Option (a) directly misstates the text of 18 U.S.C. § 17, which eliminated the volitional prong. Option (c) mistakenly imputes a total incapacity requirement to the MPC. Option (d) attributes the wrong standard of proof to the federal IDRA. Option (e) completely misstates the holding in *Kahler v. Kansas*, turning a ruling on state autonomy into a constitutional mandate against the MPC.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the federal statute adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984" — wrong because the Insanity Defense Reform Act explicitly eliminated the volitional prong from federal law.
- (c): "substantial impairment falls short of the total incapacity the Model Penal Code requires" — wrong because MPC § 4.01 explicitly requires that a defendant "lacks substantial capacity," not total incapacity.
- (d): "Under both the Model Penal Code and the Insanity Defense Reform Act, the burden rests on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence" — wrong because the IDRA statutorily requires the defendant to prove insanity by clear and convincing evidence (18 U.S.C. § 17(b)).
- (e): "ruled that due process precludes any insanity defense that extends beyond negation of mens rea" — wrong because *Kahler v. Kansas* ruled only that states are not constitutionally *required* to adopt a moral incapacity test; it did not preclude or unconstitutionalize the MPC test.

Recommended fix: none
-->
