## Q20

### Grounding

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom. <!-- correct -->

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error.

**Answer:** (d)
**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. Acquittees found not guilty by reason of insanity are ordinarily committed to secure psychiatric facilities, with duration tied to clinical status rather than the hypothetical prison sentence; in practice, commitment is frequently as long as or longer than the underlying sentence would have been. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.
**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [NGRI-civil-commitment, Mutina-instruction, release-procedures, public-misconception], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application
**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section "What Happens After Acquittal" (automatic commitment; duration tied to clinical status; judicial v. PSRB release models); Section "The Empirical Reality of the Insanity Defense" (0.26% NGRI rate; Mutina-style instruction corrective purpose).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — NGRI civil commitment and Mutina instruction, Ch 23, Refinement `ngri-civil-commitment` and `mutina-instruction`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom. <!-- correct -->

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error.

**Answer:** (d)
**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. Acquittees found not guilty by reason of insanity are ordinarily committed to secure psychiatric facilities, with duration tied to clinical status rather than the hypothetical prison sentence; in practice, commitment is frequently as long as or longer than the underlying sentence would have been. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.
**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [NGRI-civil-commitment, Mutina-instruction, release-procedures, public-misconception], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application
**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section "What Happens After Acquittal" (automatic commitment; duration tied to clinical status; judicial v. PSRB release models); Section "The Empirical Reality of the Insanity Defense" (0.26% NGRI rate; Mutina-style instruction corrective purpose).

### Edge Case Audit

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom. <!-- correct -->

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error.

**Answer:** (d)
**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom. <!-- correct -->

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error.

**Answer:** (d)
**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. Acquittees found not guilty by reason of insanity are ordinarily committed to secure psychiatric facilities, with duration tied to clinical status rather than the hypothetical prison sentence; in practice, commitment is frequently as long as or longer than the underlying sentence would have been. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.
**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [NGRI-civil-commitment, Mutina-instruction, release-procedures, public-misconception], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application
**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section "What Happens After Acquittal" (automatic commitment; duration tied to clinical status; judicial v. PSRB release models); Section "The Empirical Reality of the Insanity Defense" (0.26% NGRI rate; Mutina-style instruction corrective purpose).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that an acquittal, regardless of the underlying reason, triggers the full protection of the Double Jeopardy Clause. Under this view, once a jury returns a "not guilty" verdict, the state's criminal jurisdiction over the defendant ends immediately. Any further detention must rely on the state's parens patriae or police powers through standard civil commitment procedures, which, under Addington v. Texas, require clear and convincing evidence. Therefore, the defendant cannot be held under the umbrella of the criminal case and must be released unless separate civil proceedings are already complete.

(b) Argument-for: This option appeals to principles of proportionality and due process. A student might argue that it is fundamentally unfair to detain a person in a psychiatric facility for longer than they could have been imprisoned for the same conduct. While the Supreme Court in Jones v. United States allowed for longer commitment, a student might argue that state constitutions or modern interpretations of the Due Process Clause require a "ceiling" on commitment duration. Once the maximum penal sentence has elapsed, the state’s interest in "quasi-punitive" detention ends, necessitating release regardless of clinical status unless the state meets the higher burden of general civil commitment.

(c) Argument-for: This argument focuses on the shift toward clinical expertise in modern mental health law. A student could posit that because the determination of "dangerousness" and "recovery" is a medical rather than a legal judgment, the Constitution requires that specialized bodies (like Psychiatric Security Review Boards) manage the release process. By removing these decisions from the potentially biased or politically pressured judiciary and granting "exclusive authority" to boards of professionals, the system ensures that the acquittee’s liberty is determined by clinical reality, making any judicial override a violation of the medical-standard requirement.

(d) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the holding of Commonwealth v. Mutina, which recognized that jurors often labor under the misconception that NGRI results in immediate release. This misconception can lead to "fear-based" convictions. Moreover, it accurately reflects the holding in Jones v. United States, which permits automatic and potentially indefinite commitment of NGRI acquittees because the verdict itself establishes both mental illness and dangerousness (through the commission of the act), often leading to stays that exceed the maximum criminal sentence.

(e) Argument-for: A student might argue that the Supreme Court's decision in Kahler v. Kansas emphasized the broad discretion states have in shaping their insanity defenses, while reinforcing the traditional rule that juries should not be informed of the consequences of their verdicts. Under this interpretation, a Mutina-style instruction "improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences," which is generally prohibited in federal and many state courts (Shannon v. United States). If Kahler is read as strictly separating the jury's role as fact-finder from the dispositional consequences, then providing such instructions would constitute reversible error.

Head-to-head: (d) is the strongest answer because it accurately synthesizes the empirical reality of NGRI (long-term commitment) with the specific legal doctrine of the Mutina instruction. (a) and (b) are directly contradicted by SCOTUS in Jones v. United States, which held that NGRI acquittees are not entitled to immediate release and can be held longer than their maximum hypothetical sentence. (c) fails because the PSRB model is used in only a handful of states; most jurisdictions maintain judicial control over release. (e) is incorrect because Kahler v. Kansas dealt with the substantive elements of the insanity defense (mens rea approach vs. moral incapacity), not jury instructions regarding commitment consequences.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal... with the defendant released pending their outcome." — wrong because Jones v. United States permits automatic commitment and distinguishes NGRI from regular acquittals.
- (b): "Commitment duration... is capped at the maximum sentence... must be released regardless of clinical status." — wrong because under Jones, duration is tied to clinical status (illness and dangerousness) and can exceed the maximum sentence.
- (c): "In every jurisdiction... a psychiatric security review board... has exclusive authority... and courts are categorically barred from overriding..." — wrong because PSRBs are a minority model (e.g., Oregon) and most jurisdictions vest final authority in judges.
- (e): "The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in Kahler v. Kansas... its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error." — wrong because Kahler did not address jury instructions on consequences, and many jurisdictions continue to use or require such instructions.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom. <!-- correct -->

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error.

**Answer:** (d)
**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. Acquittees found not guilty by reason of insanity are ordinarily committed to secure psychiatric facilities, with duration tied to clinical status rather than the hypothetical prison sentence; in practice, commitment is frequently as long as or longer than the underlying sentence would have been. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.
**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [NGRI-civil-commitment, Mutina-instruction, release-procedures, public-misconception], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application
**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section "What Happens After Acquittal" (automatic commitment; duration tied to clinical status; judicial v. PSRB release models); Section "The Empirical Reality of the Insanity Defense" (0.26% NGRI rate; Mutina-style instruction corrective purpose).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: An argument for (a) relies on the basic constitutional premise that an acquittal terminates criminal jurisdiction. Because the Double Jeopardy Clause prohibits punishing someone who has been acquitted, the state loses its penal authority over an NGRI acquittee. Under this view, the state must treat the acquittee exactly like any other civilian facing civil commitment under *O'Connor v. Donaldson*, meaning they cannot be automatically confined under criminal statutes and must be released pending a separate civil hearing.
(b) Argument-for: An argument for (b) relies on principles of proportionality and due process. Because an NGRI acquittee is technically not legally culpable, it would violate equal protection to hold them for a period longer than a fully culpable convicted criminal would serve for the same act. Therefore, the maximum hypothetical criminal sentence serves as a hard constitutional cap on confinement, requiring release or a transition to standard civil commitment once that term expires.
(c) Argument-for: An argument for (c) focuses on the medicalized nature of the insanity defense. Because the NGRI verdict is predicated on a mental disease or defect, the determination of when sanity is restored is a purely clinical question. Consequently, specialized Psychiatric Security Review Boards (PSRBs) composed of mental health professionals are universally recognized as the sole competent authorities to manage release, barring lay judges from making specialized medical determinations.
(d) Argument-for: An argument for (d) is grounded in both Supreme Court precedent (*Jones v. United States*) and trial practice. *Jones* established that automatic, indefinite commitment of an NGRI acquittee is constitutional and need not be capped by the hypothetical prison sentence. Because juries often operate under the misconception that NGRI means immediate release—prompting them to convict out of fear for public safety—instructions like the Mutina instruction are given to apprise the jury of the reality of secure commitment, allowing them to make a verdict on the merits.
(e) Argument-for: An argument for (e) relies on the traditional rule that juries are fact-finders who should not be influenced by the post-verdict consequences of their decisions (as seen in *Shannon v. United States*). Introducing instructions about civil commitment inappropriately biases the jury by injecting sentencing-like outcomes into their deliberations. One could argue the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas* addressed the due process limits of the insanity defense and implicitly prohibited mechanisms that distract the jury from the core issue of moral culpability.

Head-to-head: (d) correctly states the legal holding of *Jones v. United States* regarding indefinite commitment and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. The distractors fail because they rely on explicit, falsifiable falsehoods: (a) directly contradicts *Jones* by mandating immediate release; (b) fabricates a constitutional sentence cap that *Jones* expressly rejected; (c) relies on an absolute "every jurisdiction" claim for a PSRB model that only a minority of states actually use; and (e) fabricates a holding for *Kahler v. Kansas*, which dealt with substantive due process and the abolition of the insanity defense, not jury instructions.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "results in the defendant's immediate release... must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome" — wrong because *Jones v. United States* held that automatic commitment of NGRI acquittees without a separate civil hearing is constitutional.
- (b): "capped at the maximum sentence... must be released regardless of clinical status" — wrong because the Supreme Court explicitly ruled that NGRI commitment is tied to clinical status (dangerousness and mental illness) and can exceed the maximum hypothetical sentence.
- (c): "In every jurisdiction... exclusive authority" — wrong because only a small minority of states use the PSRB model; most jurisdictions leave release decisions to judges.
- (e): "held unconstitutional in Kahler v. Kansas... its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error" — wrong because *Kahler* evaluated whether a state could abolish the moral-wrongness prong of the insanity defense, not the constitutionality of jury instructions regarding commitment.

Recommended fix: none
-->
